Ijtihad — Independent Reasoning
intermediate
6 min
The 'Illah Must Not Contradict a Companion's Saying
Condition that the 'illah must not contradict a Companion's statement, according to those who prioritize it over qiyas

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the condition that an 'illah must not contradict a Companion's saying and identify the schools that apply this condition.

Core Idea
An 'illah that contradicts a Companion's saying is invalid only for those who prioritize the saying over qiyas.

In the classical usul tradition, the validity of an 'illah (effective cause) in analogical reasoning (qiyas) is subject to several conditions. One debated condition is that the 'illah must not contradict the saying of a Companion (qawl sahabi). This condition, however, is not universally accepted; it applies only in the view of scholars who consider a Companion's statement to be a stronger source than qiyas. The Hanafi school, by contrast, generally gives precedence to qiyas over a Companion's saying, making this condition irrelevant in their framework.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Condition
Read the source text and identify the condition being discussed: the 'illah must not contradict a Companion's saying (قول صحابي).
The phrase 'لا تخالف' indicates a prohibition of contradiction.
2
Determine the Scope
Note the phrase 'عند من قدمه على القياس' — this condition applies only for those who give precedence to the Companion's saying over qiyas. For others, it is not a condition.
The condition is not absolute; it is school-dependent.
3
Compare Schools
Contrast the Hanafi position (qiyas is preferred over a single Companion's saying) with the position of other schools (e.g., some Maliki or Hanbali views) that give the Companion's saying precedence.
This difference arises from the ranking of sources in each school's usul.
4
Refer to Previous Discussion
The author says 'وقد تقدم تحقيقه' (its verification has preceded). Recognize that this is a summary point relying on an earlier detailed treatment.
Consider referring to earlier sections for a full understanding of the debate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(و) منها أن لا تخالف قول صحابي عند من قدمه) على القياس (وقد تقدم تحقيقه
(And) from them is that it does not contradict the saying of a Companion for those who give it precedence over qiyas. And its verification has preceded.
Source pages p. 340

Commentary
This condition notes that for scholars who consider the statement of a Companion as a binding source superior to qiyas, an 'illah that would contradict such a statement is invalid. The Hanafi school generally gives qiyas precedence over a Companion's saying, but some prefer the Companion's view. The author refers to a previous discussion for details.

Key Terms
قول صحابي (qawl sahabi): The legal opinion of a companion of the Prophet, considered a source by some schools.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Under what condition does an 'illah become invalid due to a Companion's statement?
A) Always, because a Companion's opinion is infallible.
B) Only for those scholars who consider the Companion's statement superior to qiyas.
C) Only if the Companion's statement is unanimous.
D) Only if the 'illah is rational.

Key Takeaway
The condition that an 'illah must not contradict a Companion's saying is not universal; it applies only in schools that prioritize qawl sahabi over qiyas, unlike the Hanafi school.

Ijtihad — Independent Reasoning
intermediate

Definition and Conditions of Ijtihad
Defining ijtihad and clarifying its scope

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the components of the Hanafi definition of ijtihad and explain why the ruling must be zanni and shar'i.

Core Idea
Ijtihad is the maximal effort of a qualified jurist to derive a speculative legal ruling, excluding certainties and rational matters.

The passage opens the conclusion on ijtihad with a precise definition. It clarifies who qualifies as a faqih, why the ruling must be shar'i and zanni, and responds to a potential objection about circularity. Understanding this definition is key to knowing when ijtihad is valid and what it produces.

Guided Path
1
Identify the components of the definition
Break down the definition: baddl al-taqa (exertion of effort), min al-faqih (by a qualified jurist), fi tahsil hukm shar'i (to obtain a legal ruling), zanni (speculative).
Each component serves to exclude something: effort excludes non-effort; faqih excludes non-jurists; shar'i excludes rational; zanni excludes certain.
2
Clarify who is a faqih
The author distinguishes a faqih as one with mastery of usul, not mere memorisation. This avoids circularity (tasalsul) because otherwise ijtihad would depend on prior ijtihad.
The objection from the commentator is refuted: the definition uses 'exertion of effort' which is more general.
3
Understand the qualification 'shar'i'
Ijtihad concerns legal rulings, not rational or scientific matters. This keeps ijtihad within the realm of Shari'ah.
Geometric or philosophical reasoning is not ijtihad in this sense.
4
Understand the qualification 'zanni'
Ijtihad yields speculative rulings, not certainties. Matters like the five pillars, prohibition of zina are qat'i and outside ijtihad.
The text explains that nazar (consideration) entails zann at least, so qat'i matters do not require ijtihad.
5
Review the objection and response
The commentator objected that faqih and mujtahid are correlated. The response: the definition uses 'exertion of effort' which is more general, and the correlation does not invalidate the definition.
The qualification 'faqih' is to exclude non-faqih's effort, not to define faqih anew.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
خاتمة
الاجتهاد بذل الطاقة من
،
الفقيه في تحصيل حكم شرعي ظني أقول : المراد من الفقيه
من أتقن لمباديه أي مبادىء الفقه بحيث يقدر على استخراجه من القوة إلى الفعل (لا المجتهد بالفعل العالم بمسائله (كما هو ظاهر المختصر) حيث قال : والفقيه ما تقدم، ومثله في شرح البديع أيضاً ( وإلا) أي إن لم يكن كذلك، بل يكون المراد المجتهد العالم بالفعل (لزم التسلسل في الاجتهاد لتوقفه على اجتهاد ،سابق ، وهو متوقف على اجتهاد آخر (ولا) أي وليس المراد من يحفظ الفروع الفقهية (فقط على ما شاع الآن لأن بذل سعيه ليس باجتهاد اصطلاحاً) وإذا عرفت هذا فقد انكشف لك حقيقة ما قالوا قيد الفقيه احتراز عن بذل الطاقة من غير الفقيه وسقط اعتراض شارح الشرح أنه لا وجه له فإنه لا يكون فقيهاً إلا بعد الاجتهاد، فبينهما تلازم، مع أنه يرد عليه أيضاً ما في «التحرير» أن التلازم بين الفقيه والمجتهد لا يضر فإنّ المذكور في التعريف بذل الطاقة وهو أعم من الاجتهاد وهذا تنزل (وإنما قيد الحكم بالشرعي لأنه المقصود ههنا وبذل الطاقة في العقليات خارج عن الاجتهاد على هذا (وأما التقيد بالظني) احترازاً عن نحو الأركان الأربعة وحرمة الزنا والشرب، والغصب، من الضروريات الدينية فمبني على أن النظرية تستلزم (الظنية وقيد النظر لا بد منه، فقيد بملزومه، والاستلزام إنما هو في الشرعيات، فلا يرد النقض بالهندسيات ( لأنها ) أي النظرية (إما لضعف دلالة المتن أو السند) فإنّ الأمر الثابت من رسول الله صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم بالتواتر مع قوة الدلالة والأحكام فيه يفيد القطع ضرورة وفيه ما فيه) لأن مبني النظرية على الخفاء، والخفي ربما يكون قطعياً فتأمل فيه (ثم قسموه أي الاجتهاد (إلى) اجتهاد (واجب) وفرض عيناً على المسؤول عن حكم (عند خوف فوت الحادثة بحيث لا يستطيع السائل السؤال من غيره قيل (و) واجب عيناً (في حق نفسه بحيث احتاج هو للعمل (و) واجب (كفاية عند عدم الخوف خوف فوت الحادثة ( وثم) مجتهد (غيره) يتمكن السائل من السؤال منه (فيأثمون بتركه ويسقط عن ذمة الكل (بفتوى أحدهم أي أحد المجتهدين لحصول المقصود (ولو ظن المجتهد الآخر (كونها) أي الفتوى (خطأ) لأن ظنه هذا لا يكون حجة على المفتي . ويحتمل الخطأ مثله، فلا يمنع العمل، فلا يجب عليه التنبيه (وإلى) اجتهاد (مندوب كالاجتهاد قبل الوقوع أي قبل وقوع الحادثة الغير المعلومة الحكم (وإلى) اجتهاد (حرام في مقابلة قاطع هذا وهذا ليس اجتهاداً حقيقة ولا يصدق الحد عليه أيضاً والتقسيم إما لأنه أريد به مطلق بذل الطاقة في استخراج حكم، أو هو كتقسيم الفرس إلى الفرس المركوب وإلى شجه المرسوم
Conclusion. Ijtihad is the exertion of maximum effort by the faqih to obtain a zanni (speculative) legal ruling. I say: The meaning of faqih is one who has mastered its principles, i.e., the principles of fiqh, such that he is able to extract it from potentiality to actuality, not the actual mujtahid who knows its issues, as is apparent from al-Mukhtasar, where it said: 'Al-faqih is what preceded.' Similarly in Sharh al-Badi'. Otherwise (i.e., if it is not so, but rather the intended meaning is the actual knowledgeable mujtahid), then there would be circularity (tasalsul) in ijtihad, because it would depend on a previous ijtihad, which itself depends on another ijtihad. And it is not meant as one who only memorizes fiqh branches, as is common nowadays, because his exertion is not ijtihad terminologically. And when you know this, the reality of what they said becomes clear: the qualification 'faqih' is to avoid (ihteraz) the exertion of effort by a non-faqih, and the objection of the commentator Sa'h al-Sharh that this is unnecessary, because one is not a faqih except after ijtihad, so there is correlation between them, is refuted. Moreover, it is also objected that in al-Tahrir, the correlation between faqih and mujtahid does not harm, because what is mentioned in the definition is 'exertion of effort' which is more general than ijtihad, and this is a lowering (tanazzul). And the ruling is qualified as shar'i because that is what is intended here, and exertion of effort in rational matters is outside ijtihad in this sense. As for the qualification 'zanni', it is to avoid matters like the five pillars and the prohibition of zina, drinking, and usurpation, which are religious certainties, based on the fact that theoretical matters entail zan, and the condition of nazar (consideration) is necessary, so it is qualified by its necessary consequent. And this entailment is only in legal matters, so the objection by geometrical matters does not apply, because they (theoretical matters) are either due to weakness of textual indication or chain. For a matter established from the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him and his family and companions) with tawatur and strong indication necessarily yields certainty, though there is something in it. Because theoretical matters are based on hiddenness, and what is hidden may sometimes be certain, so ponder. Then they divided ijtihad into: obligatory (wajib) as a personal obligation (fard 'ayn) on the one who is asked about a ruling when there is fear that the event will be missed such that the questioner cannot ask someone else (it is said also certain 'ayn in his own right when he needs to act), and communal obligation (fard kifaya) when there is no fear of missing the event and there is another mujtahid from whom the questioner can ask. So they (the scholars) sin by abandoning it, and it is lifted from all by the fatwa of one of them, because the purpose is achieved. Even if another mujtahid thinks that fatwa is wrong, his opinion is not proof against the mufti, and he is also susceptible to error, so it does not prevent action, and he is not obliged to warn. And also to recommended (mandub) ijtihad, like ijtihad before the occurrence of an event whose ruling is unknown. And to forbidden (haram) ijtihad when it opposes a definitive text. This is not really ijtihad, and the definition does not apply to it either. The division is either because by it is meant absolute exertion of effort in deriving a ruling, or it is like dividing a horse into a ridden horse and its picture (shajahu).
Source pages p. 404

Commentary
This passage opens the conclusion (khatima) on ijtihad. It defines ijtihad as the maximum effort of a faqih to derive a speculative (zanni) legal ruling. The author clarifies that 'faqih' here means one qualified in usul al-fiqh, not one who already knows all rulings by heart, to avoid circularity. The qualification of the ruling as 'shar'i' excludes rational matters, and 'zanni' excludes indisputable matters of religion. Ijtihad is then divided into three categories: obligatory (wajib), which can be personal (fard 'ayn) when a specific case must be addressed, or communal (fard kifaya) when others are available; recommended (mandub) when done proactively before an event; and forbidden (haram) when contradicting definitive texts, in which case it is not truly ijtihad. The passage also addresses an objection regarding the relationship between faqih and mujtahid, showing that the correlation does not invalidate the definition.

Key Terms
فقيه (faqih): One who has mastered the principles of fiqh such that he can derive rulings from potentiality to actuality.
اجتهاد (ijtihad): Exertion of maximum effort by a faqih to obtain a zanni legal ruling.
ظني (zanni): Speculative; not certain.
قطعي (qat'i): Certain; definitive.
متواتر (mutawatir): Mass-transmitted; a hadith transmitted by many chains ensuring certainty.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which qualification in the definition of ijtihad excludes matters like the five pillars and the prohibition of zina?
A) The qualification that the effort must be by a faqih.
B) The qualification that the ruling must be shar'i.
C) The qualification that the ruling must be zanni.
D) The qualification that the effort must be maximum.

Key Takeaway
Ijtihad is defined as the maximal effort of a qualified jurist to derive a speculative legal ruling, and it applies only to matters that are not definitively established.

Ijtihad — Independent Reasoning
intermediate
7 min
Types of Ijtihad: Wajib, Mandub, and Haram
Dividing ijtihad by legal ruling

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify ijtihad into wajib, mandub, and haram according to the Hanafi school and identify the conditions that trigger each classification.

Core Idea
Ijtihad is not always permissible; it becomes haram when it contradicts a definitive text, and its obligatory status depends on necessity and availability of other jurists.

After defining ijtihad, the passage divides it into three categories based on its legal ruling. This classification determines when a jurist must engage in ijtihad, when it is recommended, and when it is forbidden. Understanding this helps muftis prioritize their duties and avoid invalid ijtihad.

Guided Path
1
List the three categories
The passage divides ijtihad into wajib (obligatory), mandub (recommended), and haram (forbidden).
These are rulings on the act of ijtihad itself, not on the derived rulings.
2
Define obligatory ijtihad (wajib)
It becomes fard 'ayn when a jurist is asked and there is fear of the event being missed, and the questioner cannot find another mujtahid. It is also fard 'ayn for himself when he needs to act. It is fard kifaya when no fear of missing and others are available.
The difference is based on need and availability.
3
Define recommended ijtihad (mandub)
It is mandub when done proactively before the occurrence of an event whose ruling is unknown.
Example: researching hypothetical issues for future reference.
4
Define forbidden ijtihad (haram)
It is haram when it contradicts a definitive text (qati'). In this case, it is not considered true ijtihad and the definition does not apply.
Such effort is invalid and sinful.
5
Understand the ruling on differing fatwas
If one mufti gives a fatwa, another mufti who thinks it is wrong is not obliged to warn, because his opinion is also speculative and not proof against the first.
This allows multiple valid ijtihad opinions to coexist.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Commentary
This passage opens the conclusion (khatima) on ijtihad. It defines ijtihad as the maximum effort of a faqih to derive a speculative (zanni) legal ruling. The author clarifies that 'faqih' here means one qualified in usul al-fiqh, not one who already knows all rulings by heart, to avoid circularity. The qualification of the ruling as 'shar'i' excludes rational matters, and 'zanni' excludes indisputable matters of religion. Ijtihad is then divided into three categories: obligatory (wajib), which can be personal (fard 'ayn) when a specific case must be addressed, or communal (fard kifaya) when others are available; recommended (mandub) when done proactively before an event; and forbidden (haram) when contradicting definitive texts, in which case it is not truly ijtihad. The passage also addresses an objection regarding the relationship between faqih and mujtahid, showing that the correlation does not invalidate the definition.

Key Terms
فقيه (faqih): One who has mastered the principles of fiqh such that he can derive rulings from potentiality to actuality.
اجتهاد واجب (ijtihad wajib): Obligatory ijtihad, divided into fard 'ayn and fard kifaya under specific conditions.
اجتهاد مندوب (ijtihad mandub): Recommended ijtihad, done proactively before a case occurs.
اجتهاد حرام (ijtihad haram): Forbidden ijtihad that contradicts a definitive text.

Checkpoint
Which type of ijtihad is considered haram according to this passage?
A) Ijtihad when the event is not yet occurred.
B) Ijtihad in opposition to a definitive text.
C) Ijtihad when there is fear of missing the event.
D) Ijtihad by a non-faqih.

Key Takeaway
Ijtihad is classified as wajib (personal or communal), mandub, or haram based on necessity, availability, and compliance with definitive texts.

Classifying Ijtihad: Obligatory, Recommended, and Prohibited
Classifying ijtihad into obligatory, recommended, and prohibited, and mujtahid into absolute and partial

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify ijtihad into wajib, mandub, and haram based on conditions, and distinguish between a mujtahid mutlaq and a mujtahid fi al-ba'd.

Core Idea
Ijtihad is not always permissible—it becomes haram when contradicting a definitive proof.

This passage from Fawatih al-Rahamut explains the legal categories of ijtihad and the types of mujtahids. Understanding when ijtihad is obligatory, recommended, or prohibited is essential for applying usul al-fiqh correctly, especially in determining the duty of a scholar in a given situation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three categories of ijtihad
List ijtihad wajib, ijtihad mandub, and ijtihad haram as mentioned in the text.
Ijtihad wajib is further divided into fard 'ayn and fard kifayah.
2
Analyze the conditions for each category
For wajib: check if the incident will be missed, if the questioner can ask another, and if the scholar himself needs to act. For mandub: occurs before the incident. For haram: when opposing a qati (definitive proof).
3
Recognize the two types of mujtahid
Mujtahid mutlaq (absolute) can perform ijtihad in every incident; mujtahid fi al-ba'd (partial) only in some issues.
The text promises a later discussion on partial mujtahid.
4
Understand the nature of prohibited ijtihad
Ijtihad haram is not considered true ijtihad; it does not satisfy the definition because it contradicts a definitive proof.
The division is either for absolute exertion or a metaphorical classification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم قسموه أي الاجتهاد (إلى) اجتهاد (واجب) وفرض عيناً على المسؤول عن حكم (عند خوف فوت الحادثة بحيث لا يستطيع السائل السؤال من غيره قيل (و) واجب عيناً (في حق نفسه بحيث احتاج هو للعمل (و) واجب (كفاية عند عدم الخوف خوف فوت الحادثة ( وثم) مجتهد (غيره) يتمكن السائل من السؤال منه (فيأثمون بتركه ويسقط عن ذمة الكل (بفتوى أحدهم أي أحد المجتهدين لحصول المقصود (ولو ظن المجتهد الآخر (كونها) أي الفتوى (خطأ) لأن ظنه هذا لا يكون حجة على المفتي . ويحتمل الخطأ مثله، فلا يمنع العمل، فلا يجب عليه التنبيه (وإلى) اجتهاد (مندوب كالاجتهاد قبل الوقوع أي قبل وقوع الحادثة الغير المعلومة الحكم (وإلى) اجتهاد (حرام في مقابلة قاطع هذا وهذا ليس اجتهاداً حقيقة ولا يصدق الحد عليه أيضاً والتقسيم إما لأنه أريد به مطلق بذل الطاقة في استخراج حكم، أو هو كتقسيم الفرس إلى الفرس المركوب وإلى شجه [؟] المرسوم بعد على اللوح ثم المجتهد على قسمين مجتهد مطلق أي من له قدرة الاجتهاد في كل حادثة اتفقت ومجتهد في البعض وسيجيء حاله
Then they divided ijtihad into: obligatory (wajib) — which is a fard 'ayn on the one asked about a ruling when there is fear of the incident being missed such that the questioner cannot ask someone else; and it is said it is also a fard 'ayn on a person himself when he needs to act; and it is a fard kifayah when there is no fear of missing the incident and there is another mujtahid from whom the questioner can ask. They are sinful if they abandon it, but it is lifted from all of them by the fatwa of one of them, because the purpose is achieved. Even if another mujtahid thinks the fatwa is wrong, his opinion is not a proof against the mufti; it could also be wrong, so it does not prevent action, and he is not required to point it out. And (they divided it into) recommended (mandub) ijtihad, like ijtihad before an event occurs whose ruling is unknown. And (they divided it into) prohibited (haram) ijtihad in opposition to a definitive proof. This and that are not truly ijtihad, nor does the definition apply to them. The division is either because they intended by it absolute exertion of effort to extract a ruling, or it is like dividing a horse into a ridden horse and a drawn horse on a board. Then the mujtahid is of two types: absolute mujtahid, i.e., one who has the ability to perform ijtihad in every incident that arises, and partial mujtahid (in some issues). His situation will come later.
Source pages p. 404

Commentary
This passage discusses the different rulings (ahkam) that ijtihad can take: sometimes it is obligatory upon an individual (fard 'ayn) when there is an urgent need and no other source; sometimes it is a communal obligation (fard kifayah); sometimes it is recommended, e.g., studying future hypotheticals; and sometimes it is prohibited if it contradicts a definitive text. The author also notes that such 'prohibited ijtihad' is not really ijtihad in the true sense. Finally, mujtahids are divided into two categories: the absolute mujtahid who can rule on all issues, and the partial mujtahid who specializes in certain areas (like fara'id).

Key Terms
اجتهاد واجب (ijtihad wajib): الاجتهاد الذي يكون فرض عين أو كفاية بحسب الحال (ijtihad that is individually or communally obligatory depending on circumstances)
اجتهاد مندوب (ijtihad mandub): الاجتهاد قبل وقوع الحادثة (ijtihad before an event occurs, for hypothetical study)
اجتهاد حرام (ijtihad haram): الاجتهاد في مقابلة قاطع (ijtihad against a definitive proof)
مجتهد مطلق (mujtahid mutlaq): من له قدرة الاجتهاد في كل حادثة (one who has the ability to perform ijtihad in every incident)
مجتهد في البعض (mujtahid fi al-ba'd): من له قدرة الاجتهاد في بعض المسائل دون بعض (one who can perform ijtihad in some issues but not others)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When is ijtihad considered haram according to this passage?
A) When the mujtahid is not absolutely qualified
B) When it is done before the incident occurs
C) When it is in opposition to a definitive proof (qati)
D) When another mujtahid already gave a fatwa

Key Takeaway
Ijtihad is only valid within the bounds of definitive proofs; opposing a qati renders it prohibited and not true ijtihad.

Essential Conditions of a Mujtahid
Listing prerequisites for a mujtahid

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the required conditions for a mujtahid according to the Hanafi school and explain why 'adalah is not a condition for ijtihad itself.

Core Idea
A mujtahid must know Quran, Sunnah, Ijma, Usul, and Arabic; 'adalah is only needed for fatwa acceptance.

In this passage, the author outlines the qualifications a mujtahid must possess. These include sound faith, knowledge of the Quran and Sunnah (to a sufficient extent), knowledge of ijma', familiarity with usul al-fiqh, and enough Arabic. Importantly, the text distinguishes between conditions for the validity of ijtihad and conditions for the acceptance of the fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify each condition and its scope
Read the list: sound faith, knowledge of Allah and attributes, knowledge of Quran (about 500 relevant verses), knowledge of Sunnah text and chain (about 1200 hadith), knowledge of ijma', knowledge of usul al-fiqh, and sufficient Arabic.
Note that full mastery of each field is not required.
2
Understand the scope of Quran knowledge
The text states that knowing the entire Quran is not required; only the amount related to rulings (estimated at 500 verses).
3
Analyze the role of 'adalah
The text explicitly states that 'adalah (moral integrity) is a condition for accepting the fatwa, not for the actual ijtihad. A fasiq can still perform ijtihad but his fatwa is not accepted.
This is a distinctive Hanafi position.
4
Recognize that these conditions are for absolute ijtihad
The conditions are for the mujtahid mutlaq (absolute mujtahid). Later, the discussion on partial ijtihad allows specialization.

(مسألة: اختلف في تجزي الاجتهاد) بأن يكون مجتهداً في بعض المسائل دون بعض ويتفرع عليه اجتهاد الفرضي أي من له معرفة في نصوص فرائض السهام، والآثار الواردة فيها (في الفرائض) يجتهد (فقط) دون غيرها من الأحكام (فالأكثر) قالوا (نعم) يتجزى الاجتهاد (ومنهم) الإمام حجة الإسلام (الغزالي قدس سره من الشافعية (و) الشيخ ابن الهمام) رحمه الله منا، ويلوح رضا صاحب البديع» به أيضاً (وهو الأشبه بالصواب (وقيل: لا يتجزى، (وتوقف ابن الحاجب، لنا كما أقول أولاً : ترك العلم الحاصل عن دليل إلى تقليد) وهو ليس بعلم حقيقة خلاف المعقول) فلا يلتفت إليه (كيف) وفيه أي في التقليد (ريب) عند المقلد هل هو مطابق أم لا، وما عن الدليل خال عن هذا الريب. وقد قال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم : دع ما يريبك إلى ما لا يريبك و لنا (ثانياً) قوله : («استفت نفسك وإن أفتاك المفتون ففيه ترجيح اجتهاده على اجتهاد غيره) حيث أمر بالاستفتاء من ولنا ثالثاً : أن المجتهد في البعض يعرف حكمه عن دليل منصوب من قبل الشارع، فيحصل له معرفة حكم الله تعالى، فيجب اتباعه ولا يسوغ تركه بقول أحد فإنا إنما أمرنا بالاتباع لقول رسول الله ، واتباع غيره بظن أنه حاك، فإذا علم حكم من قوله صلى الله عليه وسلم فقد ظن أن ما وراءه
And its condition—i.e., the condition of the mujtahid when he is absolute—is: soundness of faith, because it is a condition for every act of worship, and also ijtihad is extraction of a ruling, so knowledge of the Ruler (Allah) and of the means of conveying rulings and His other attributes—power, knowledge, will, speech, wisdom, etc.—is necessary, even through general proofs. That is, his knowledge of the detailed proofs mentioned in kalam to repel the doubts of obstinate disputants is not a condition. And knowledge of the Book (Quran)—its text, meaning, and ruling—because it is the foundation of rulings. But knowledge of the entire Book is not a condition; rather, the amount that relates to rulings. And it is said: about five hundred verses. And after that, knowledge of the Sunnah in terms of text—knowing its meaning and method of interpretation. But knowledge of all sunan is not a condition; rather, the amount around which most rulings revolve. It is said: about one thousand two hundred. And knowledge of the Sunnah in terms of chain—knowing whether it is mutawatir, mashhur, or ahad, with knowledge of the condition of narrators. Otherwise, he cannot distinguish sound from unsound, and the basis of the ruling is not clear. Even through transmission from leading scholars, it means he does not have to know personally by accompanying them. And after that, knowledge of the locations of ijma, lest he make ijtihad in opposition to it while it is definitive. And that he should have a good share of knowledge from what this science (usul al-fiqh) deals with; although its codification is recent, the codified (as a passive participle) is prior, because the methods of extracting rulings become clear from it. Then he must have knowledge of sarf, nahw, and language, but to the extent that he can understand the meanings of the Book and Sunnah, not like al-Asma'i, al-Khalil, and Sibawayh. As for 'adalah (integrity), it is a condition for the acceptance of the fatwa, because a fasiq (sinner) necessitates pause in his report of the text, but it is not a condition for the actual validity of ijtihad, as is not hidden. Issue: There is disagreement about the divisibility of ijtihad—that one can be a mujtahid in some issues but not others. From this branches the ijtihad of a faradi (inheritance specialist): he performs ijtihad only in fara'id, based on texts about shares and the narrations therein, not in other rulings. The majority said yes, ijtihad can be partial. Among them are Imam al-Ghazali (from the Shafi'is) and Shaykh Ibn al-Humam (from us), and the author of al-Badi' seems to agree. And it is closer to correctness. It is said no, it cannot be partial, and Ibn al-Hajib hesitated. Our evidence: First, abandoning knowledge obtained from proof to taqlid (is not actually knowledge, contrary to reason), so it is not considered. How so? In taqlid there is doubt (about whether it is correct or not), whereas what comes from proof is free of this doubt. The Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him and his family and companions, said: 'Leave what makes you doubt for what does not make you doubt.' Second, his saying: 'Consult your own heart, even if muftis give you fatwas'—this gives preference to one's own ijtihad over others' ijtihad. Third, the partial mujtahid knows his ruling from a proof established by the Lawgiver, so he obtains knowledge of Allah's ruling, so it is obligatory to follow it, and it is not permissible to leave it based on anyone's statement. We are only commanded to follow the statement of the Messenger of Allah, and following others is based on the presumption that it represents (the truth). So if he knows the ruling from the Prophet's statement, then he has come to know...
Source pages p. 405

Commentary
This passage first lists the necessary qualifications for a mujtahid: correct faith, knowledge of Allah and His attributes (at a general level), knowledge of the Quran (about 500 relevant verses), knowledge of the Sunnah in text and chain (about 1200 hadith), knowledge of ijmā', and knowledge of usul al-fiqh, along with enough Arabic grammar and language to understand the texts. Significantly, the author states that 'adalah (moral integrity) is a condition for accepting a fatwa but not for the validity of ijtihad itself—a fasiq can still be a mujtahid but his fatwa may not be trustworthy. Then the text enters a dispute (mas'alah) about whether ijtihad can be partial (tajzi' al-ijtihad). The majority, including Ghazali and Ibn al-Humam, allow it, such as a specialist in inheritance law (faradi) who exercises ijtihad only in that field. The author gives three proofs: (1) Taqlid involves doubt while ijtihad has none, and the hadith urges leaving doubt. (2) The hadith 'Consult your heart' shows reliance on one's own ijtihad. (3) A partial mujtahid who derives a ruling directly from the text must follow it, not abandon it for others' opinions.

Key Terms
صحة الإيمان (sihhat al-iman): شرط في المجتهد أن يكون صحيح الإيمان بالله وصفاته (soundness of faith is a necessary condition for a mujtahid)
عدالة (adalah): شرط لقبول الفتوى لا لتحقق الاجتهاد (integrity is a condition for accepting the fatwa, not for the actual ijtihad)

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Sound faith

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school sets minimal yet comprehensive conditions for ijtihad, with the key nuance that moral integrity is only required for fatwa acceptance, not for the act of ijtihad.

The Debate on Partial Ijtihad (Tajzi' al-Ijtihad)
Arguing for the permissibility of partial ijtihad

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on tajzi' al-ijtihad, cite the three proofs from the text, and identify the practical consequence for specialists like the faradi.

Core Idea
Partial ijtihad is allowed: a scholar can be a mujtahid in inheritance law alone, supported by hadith and the nature of proof-based knowledge.

This passage presents a key dispute (mas'alah) in usul al-fiqh: can ijtihad be divided into specializations? The majority of scholars, including Hanafis, permit it, while some like Ibn al-Hajib hesitated. The author provides three proofs from the hadith and logic to defend partial ijtihad, and cites the example of the faradi (inheritance specialist) who performs ijtihad only in fara'id.

Guided Path
1
State the issue and the opposing views
The issue is: can a person be a mujtahid in some areas (e.g., inheritance) but not others? The majority say yes; some say no or hesitate.
2
Identify the first proof
First proof: Taqlid involves doubt, while ijtihad based on proof is free from doubt. The hadith 'Leave what makes you doubt for what does not make you doubt' supports following one's own ijtihad even if partial.
Hadith: 'دع ما يريبك إلى ما لا يريبك'
3
Identify the second proof
Second proof: The hadith 'Consult your own heart, even if muftis give you fatwas' shows preference for one's own ijtihad, implying it can be exercised partially.
Hadith: 'استفت نفسك وإن أفتاك المفتون'
4
Identify the third proof
Third proof: A partial mujtahid who knows the ruling from a direct proof from the Lawgiver must follow it; he cannot abandon it for another's opinion. This establishes the permissibility of partial ijtihad.
This is a logical proof based on obligation to follow proof.
5
Understand the practical consequence
The faradi (inheritance specialist) can exercise ijtihad in fara'id only, deriving rulings directly from texts on shares, without needing to be a mujtahid in other areas.
This is the thamarah al-khilaf.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وشرطه) أي شرط المجتهد حال كونه (مطلقاً صحة إيمانه) فإنه شرط في كل عباده وأيضاً الاجتهاد استخراج الحكم، فلا بد من معرفة الحاكم ومن هو وسيلة في تبليغ الأحكام وسائر صفاته من القدرة والعلم والإرادة والكلام والحكمة ونحوها ولو بالأدلة الإجمالية) يعني معرفته الأدلة التفصيلية المذكورة في علم الكلام، بحيث يقدر على دفع شبه المكابرين المجادلين ليست شرطاً (ومعرفة الكتاب) متناً ومعنى وحكماً، لأنه أساس الأحكام، ثم معرفة الكتاب كله ليست شرطاً، بل القدر الذي له تعلق بالأحكام وإلى تقديره أشار بقوله ( وقيل : بقدر خمسمائة آية و بعد معرفة السنة متناً) فيعلم معناه وطريق تأويله، ثم ليس معرفته جميع السنن شرطاً، بل القدر الذي يدور عليه أكثر الأحكام (قيل: التي يدور عليها العلم ألف ومائتان و معرفة السنة (سنداً) بأن يعلم تواتره أو شهرته أو سندها التي رويت به آحاداً (مع العلم بحال الرواة) وإلا لم يتميز عنده الصحيح عن السقيم فلا يظهر مأخذ الحكم ولو بالنقل عن أئمة الشأن يعني لا يشترط معرفته بنفسه بملازمته إياهم (و) بعد معرفته (مواقع الإجماع) لئلا يجتهد مخالفاً له مع كونه قطعياً (أن يكون) خبر لقوله وشرطه أي شرطه بعد هذه الشروط أن يكون ذا حظ وافر من العلم (مما تصدى له هذا العلم علم الأصول فإنّ تدوينه وإن كان حادثاً لكن المدوّن بصيغة المفعول (سابق) لأنّ طرق استخراج الأحكام إما تتبين منه، ثم لا بد له من معرفة الصرف والنحو واللغة، لكن بقدر ما يتمكن به من معرفة معاني الكتاب والسنة لا كونه مثل الأصمعي والخليل وسيبويه (وأما العدالة فشرط قبول (الفتوى فإنّ الفاسق واجب التوقف في إخباره بالنص وليس شرطاً في نفس تحقق الاجتهاد كما لا يخفى.

(مسألة: اختلف في تجزي الاجتهاد) بأن يكون مجتهداً في بعض المسائل دون بعض ويتفرع عليه اجتهاد الفرضي أي من له معرفة في نصوص فرائض السهام، والآثار الواردة فيها (في الفرائض) يجتهد (فقط) دون غيرها من الأحكام (فالأكثر) قالوا (نعم) يتجزى الاجتهاد (ومنهم) الإمام حجة الإسلام (الغزالي قدس سره من الشافعية (و) الشيخ ابن الهمام) رحمه الله منا، ويلوح رضا صاحب البديع» به أيضاً (وهو الأشبه بالصواب (وقيل: لا يتجزى، (وتوقف ابن الحاجب، لنا كما أقول أولاً : ترك العلم الحاصل عن دليل إلى تقليد) وهو ليس بعلم حقيقة خلاف المعقول) فلا يلتفت إليه (كيف) وفيه أي في التقليد (ريب) عند المقلد هل هو مطابق أم لا، وما عن الدليل خال عن هذا الريب. وقد قال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم : دع ما يريبك إلى ما لا يريبك و لنا (ثانياً) قوله : («استفت نفسك وإن أفتاك المفتون ففيه ترجيح اجتهاده على اجتهاد غيره) حيث أمر بالاستفتاء من ولنا ثالثاً : أن المجتهد في البعض يعرف حكمه عن دليل منصوب من قبل الشارع، فيحصل له معرفة حكم الله تعالى، فيجب اتباعه ولا يسوغ تركه بقول أحد فإنا إنما أمرنا بالاتباع لقول رسول الله ، واتباع غيره بظن أنه حاك، فإذا علم حكم من قوله صلى الله عليه وسلم فقد ظن أن ما وراءه
And its condition—i.e., the condition of the mujtahid when he is absolute—is: soundness of faith, because it is a condition for every act of worship, and also ijtihad is extraction of a ruling, so knowledge of the Ruler (Allah) and of the means of conveying rulings and His other attributes—power, knowledge, will, speech, wisdom, etc.—is necessary, even through general proofs. That is, his knowledge of the detailed proofs mentioned in kalam to repel the doubts of obstinate disputants is not a condition. And knowledge of the Book (Quran)—its text, meaning, and ruling—because it is the foundation of rulings. But knowledge of the entire Book is not a condition; rather, the amount that relates to rulings. And it is said: about five hundred verses. And after that, knowledge of the Sunnah in terms of text—knowing its meaning and method of interpretation. But knowledge of all sunan is not a condition; rather, the amount around which most rulings revolve. It is said: about one thousand two hundred. And knowledge of the Sunnah in terms of chain—knowing whether it is mutawatir, mashhur, or ahad, with knowledge of the condition of narrators. Otherwise, he cannot distinguish sound from unsound, and the basis of the ruling is not clear. Even through transmission from leading scholars, it means he does not have to know personally by accompanying them. And after that, knowledge of the locations of ijma, lest he make ijtihad in opposition to it while it is definitive. And that he should have a good share of knowledge from what this science (usul al-fiqh) deals with; although its codification is recent, the codified (as a passive participle) is prior, because the methods of extracting rulings become clear from it. Then he must have knowledge of sarf, nahw, and language, but to the extent that he can understand the meanings of the Book and Sunnah, not like al-Asma'i, al-Khalil, and Sibawayh. As for 'adalah (integrity), it is a condition for the acceptance of the fatwa, because a fasiq (sinner) necessitates pause in his report of the text, but it is not a condition for the actual validity of ijtihad, as is not hidden. Issue: There is disagreement about the divisibility of ijtihad—that one can be a mujtahid in some issues but not others. From this branches the ijtihad of a faradi (inheritance specialist): he performs ijtihad only in fara'id, based on texts about shares and the narrations therein, not in other rulings. The majority said yes, ijtihad can be partial. Among them are Imam al-Ghazali (from the Shafi'is) and Shaykh Ibn al-Humam (from us), and the author of al-Badi' seems to agree. And it is closer to correctness. It is said no, it cannot be partial, and Ibn al-Hajib hesitated. Our evidence: First, abandoning knowledge obtained from proof to taqlid (is not actually knowledge, contrary to reason), so it is not considered. How so? In taqlid there is doubt (about whether it is correct or not), whereas what comes from proof is free of this doubt. The Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him and his family and companions, said: 'Leave what makes you doubt for what does not make you doubt.' Second, his saying: 'Consult your own heart, even if muftis give you fatwas'—this gives preference to one's own ijtihad over others' ijtihad. Third, the partial mujtahid knows his ruling from a proof established by the Lawgiver, so he obtains knowledge of Allah's ruling, so it is obligatory to follow it, and it is not permissible to leave it based on anyone's statement. We are only commanded to follow the statement of the Messenger of Allah, and following others is based on the presumption that it represents (the truth). So if he knows the ruling from the Prophet's statement, then he has come to know...
Source pages p. 405

Commentary
This passage first lists the necessary qualifications for a mujtahid: correct faith, knowledge of Allah and His attributes (at a general level), knowledge of the Quran (about 500 relevant verses), knowledge of the Sunnah in text and chain (about 1200 hadith), knowledge of ijmā', and knowledge of usul al-fiqh, along with enough Arabic grammar and language to understand the texts. Significantly, the author states that 'adalah (moral integrity) is a condition for accepting a fatwa but not for the validity of ijtihad itself—a fasiq can still be a mujtahid but his fatwa may not be trustworthy. Then the text enters a dispute (mas'alah) about whether ijtihad can be partial (tajzi' al-ijtihad). The majority, including Ghazali and Ibn al-Humam, allow it, such as a specialist in inheritance law (faradi) who exercises ijtihad only in that field. The author gives three proofs: (1) Taqlid involves doubt while ijtihad has none, and the hadith urges leaving doubt. (2) The hadith 'Consult your heart' shows reliance on one's own ijtihad. (3) A partial mujtahid who derives a ruling directly from the text must follow it, not abandon it for others' opinions.

Key Terms
تجزي الاجتهاد (tajzi' al-ijtihad): أن يكون مجتهداً في بعض المسائل دون بعض (being a mujtahid in some issues but not others)
فرضي (faradi): من له معرفة في نصوص فرائض السهام والآثار الواردة فيها (specialist in inheritance shares and related narrations)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT one of the three proofs for partial ijtihad mentioned in the text?
A) The hadith 'Leave what makes you doubt'
B) The hadith 'Consult your own heart'
C) The analogy that a faradi is like a full mujtahid
D) The logical proof that a partial mujtahid must follow his own derived ruling

Key Takeaway
Partial ijtihad is permissible according to the majority of Hanafi and Shafi'i scholars, allowing specialization in fields like inheritance law.

Ijtihad — Independent Reasoning
advanced
12 min
The Permissibility of Partial Ijtihad (Tajazzu al-Ijtihad)
Dispute over partial ijtihad with proofs and counter-arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on partial ijtihad, identify the proofs for and against it, and evaluate the opposing argument.

Core Idea
Ijtihad can be partial: a scholar may be a mujtahid in one field while following taqlid in others.

This passage from Fawatih al-Rahamut addresses a fundamental usul question: must a mujtahid be competent in all areas of fiqh, or can one specialize in a single field like inheritance (fara'id)? The author favors the majority view that partial ijtihad is valid, citing hadith and rational arguments. Understanding this dispute prepares you to evaluate the scope of ijtihad and the validity of specialized scholarship in the Hanafi tradition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dispute and the Hanafi stance
Read the opening lines: the text states 'They differed over the permissibility of partial ijtihad.' Note that the majority (including al-Ghazali and Ibn al-Humam) say yes; Ibn al-Hajib hesitated. The author endorses the view that partiality is 'al-ashbah bi al-sawab' (closer to correctness).
The Hanafi position is clearly in favor of tajazzu.
2
Extract the first proof: the hadith 'Leave what brings you doubt'
The author argues that taqlid involves doubt about whether the fatwa is correct, while ijtihad from proof is free from doubt. Connect this to the hadith: 'Leave what brings you doubt for what does not bring you doubt.'
This is a dalil naqli (textual proof) of ahad category.
3
Extract the second proof: the hadith 'Consult your own heart'
The text cites 'Consult your own heart even if people give you fatwas' to show that one's own ijtihad is preferred over following others. This supports the validity of personal ijtihad, even if partial.
The command to consult oneself implies that one can have valid ijtihad in at least some matters.
4
Analyze the third proof: the authority of the partial mujtahid
The author argues that a partial mujtahid knows the ruling from a proof set by the Lawgiver, so his ruling must be followed. Once he knows the Prophet's statement, he cannot prefer anyone else's opinion.
This establishes that knowledge from proof creates binding authority, regardless of the mujtahid's scope.
5
Evaluate the opposing argument and the Hanafi response
The opponents say that a partial mujtahid may lack necessary knowledge for a given ruling, so his ijtihad is invalid. The Hanafi response: the assumption is that he has all necessary knowledge in his opinion; remote possibilities do not harm the dominant assumption (zann). The text also argues that if the opposing view were accepted, it would lead to absurd consequences like equality of all mujtahids in all fields.
Notice the use of 'al-zann al-ghalib' as the basis for ijtihad; perfect certainty is not required.

مسألة: اختلف في تجزي الاجتهاد بأن يكون مجتهداً في بعض المسائل دون بعض ويتفرع عليه اجتهاد الفرضي أي من له معرفة في نصوص فرائض السهام، والآثار الواردة فيها في الفرائض يجتهد فقط دون غيرها من الأحكام فالأكثر قالوا نعم يتجزى الاجتهاد ومنهم الإمام حجة الإسلام الغزالي قدس سره من الشافعية والشيخ ابن الهمام رحمه الله منا، ويلوح رضا صاحب البديع به أيضاً وهو الأشبه بالصواب وقيل: لا يتجزى، وتوقف ابن الحاجب، لنا أولاً : ترك العلم الحاصل عن دليل إلى تقليد وهو ليس بعلم حقيقة خلاف المعقول فلا يلتفت إليه كيف وفيه أي في التقليد ريب عند المقلد هل هو مطابق أم لا، وما عن الدليل خال عن هذا الريب. وقد قال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم : دع ما يريبك إلى ما لا يريبك و لنا ثانياً قوله : «استفت نفسك وإن أفتاك المفتون ففيه ترجيح اجتهاده على اجتهاد غيره» حيث أمر بالاستفتاء من نفسه و لنا ثالثاً : أن المجتهد في البعض يعرف حكمه عن دليل منصوب من قبل الشارع، فيحصل له معرفة حكم الله تعالى، فيجب اتباعه ولا يسوغ تركه بقول أحد فإنا إنما أمرنا بالاتباع لقول رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم واتباع غيره بظن أنه حاك، فإذا علم حكم من قوله صلى الله عليه وسلم فقد ظن أن ما وراءه مخالف لحكمه فيحرم اتباعه، ومن له حسن أدب بأحكام الله تعالى لا يتعدى عن هذا الأصل فافهم. واستدل على المختار أولاً لو لم يتجز الاجتهاد وانحصر في اجتهاد المجتهد في الكل لعلم أي لزم للمجتهد العلم بجميع المآخذ للأحكام كلها فعلم بجميع الأحكام وهو باطل قطعاً، فالاجتهاد متجز وأجيب بمنع الملازمة الثانية لجواز التوقف أي لجواز توقف معرفة بعض الأحكام على الاجتهاد في المآخذ ولا يكفي العلم بالمآخذ فقط وعدم المانع من التعارض وغيره. أقول : ولك أن تمنع الملازمة الأولى وهي لزوم معرفة جميع المآخذ لعدم التجزىء لأن أمير المؤمنين عمر رضي الله عنه وغيره من الصحابة رضي الله تعالى عنهم اجتهدوا في مسائل كثيرة لم يستحضروا فيها النصوص الواردة حتى رويت تلك النصوص لهم فرجعوا إليها وهذا غير وافٍ فإنه إن جهل مأخذ بعض الأحكام فلا يتمكن من استخراجها منه، فلم يكن مجتهداً في الكل، ومعرفة المأخذ عبارة عن معرفة أمور يتمكن بها من استخراج حكم واقعة. وأمير المؤمنين وإن كان لم يستحضر بعض الأحاديث لكنه كان يعلم أموراً يتمكن بها من استخراج حكم ما ورد فيه الخبر ولو بالقياس، نعم : بعض الصحابة أفتوا ببعض المسائل، وفي بعضها احتاجوا إلى الغيرة، فإما للتعارض أو نحوه من الموانع، وإلا فهو من دلائل تجزي الاجتهاد كما حكى صاحب الكشف عن الإمام حجة الإسلام فافهم. و استدل على المختار ثانياً : إذا حصل ما يتعلق بمسألة مما يتوقف عليه تحصيلها لأحد فهو وغيره ممن حصل له ما يتعلق بالمسائل كلها فيها أي في تلك المسألة سواء كما أنه يتمكن من استخراجها كذلك يتمكن ذلك، فيقبل قول ذلك ويحرم التقليد، كما يقبل قول هذا التقليد والمزية في غيرها لهذا لا دخل له فيها أي في تلك المسألة وأجيب بمنع الاستواء بينهما في استخراج تلك المسألة كيف فقد يكون ما لم يعلمه متعلقاً بها فلا يتمكن من استخراجها، فإن قلت : فالمجتهد المطلق أيضاً غير عالم بالجميع بالضرورة فيجوز أن يكون لما لا يعلمه تعلق بجميع المسائل فلا يصح اجتهاد قال : وهذا أي التعلق لما لا يعلمه بها غير ظاهر في المجتهد المطلق بل الظاهر عدم التعلق. والحق أن إبداء هذا الاحتمال في المجتهد في البعض أيضاً أبعد لا يلتفت إليه المنكرون قالوا: كل ما يقدر جهله به للمجتهد في البعض يجوز تعلقه بالحكم فلا يتمكن من استخراجه الحكم، فلا يصح الاجتهاد فيه قلنا: المفروض فيه حصول جميع ما يتعلق به في ظنه ولو كان هذا الجميع بتقرير الأئمة، والاحتمال البعيد الذي ذكرتم لا يقدح في الظن وعليه المدار، فإن قلت: قد سلم المصنف في جواب استدلالهم الثاني احتمال التعلق وههنا منع . قلت : هناك كان في جواب الاستدلال ويكفي للمنع الاحتمال وههنا الاحتياج إلى هذه المقدمة لإتمام الدليل، ولا يكفيه الاحتمال، والحق غير خاف عليك أقول : وأيضاً لو تم هذا الدليل لكان كل مجتهد مساوياً لكل في كل باب في العلم، وإلا لكان البعض مجهولاً للبعض، ويجوز أن يكون له دخل في المسائل فلا يصح واللازم باطل وكيف ويلزم أن يكون ابن شريح وأبو ثور هذا ويحرم
Issue: They differed over the permissibility of partial ijtihad, that one is a mujtahid in some issues but not others. It branches from it the ijtihad of a faraidi, meaning one who has knowledge of the texts of obligatory shares and the reports related to them, he performs ijtihad in inheritance only, not in other rulings. The majority said yes, ijtihad is partial. Among them are Imam Hujjat al-Islam al-Ghazali (may his secret be sanctified) from the Shafi'is, and Shaykh Ibn al-Humam (may Allah have mercy on him) from us, and the author of al-Badi' seems to approve of it as well. It is the more likely correct. And it was said: no partiality. Ibn al-Hajib hesitated. Our proof first: leaving knowledge obtained from a proof for taqlid is not truly knowledge, contrary to reason, so it is not heeded. How? Because in taqlid there is doubt (rayb) for the muqallid whether it is correct or not, while what is from the proof is free from this doubt. And the Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'Leave what causes you doubt for what does not cause you doubt.' Our proof second: his statement: 'Consult your own heart even if people give you fatwas' — in it is a preference for one's own ijtihad over that of others, as he commanded to seek a fatwa from oneself. Our proof third: that the mujtahid in part knows his ruling from a proof set by the Lawgiver, so he obtains knowledge of Allah's ruling, so following it is obligatory and it is not permissible to leave it for anyone's statement. For we are commanded to follow the statement of the Prophet (peace be upon him), and following others is based on the assumption that they convey. So when he knows a ruling from his statement, he assumes that what is behind it contradicts his ruling, so following it is prohibited. And one who has good manners towards Allah's rulings does not deviate from this principle, so understand. And he argued for the chosen view first: if ijtihad were not partial and limited to the ijtihad of the complete mujtahid, it would require knowledge of all sources for all rulings, thus knowledge of all rulings, which is absolutely false, so ijtihad is partial. And it was answered by denying the second entailment because of the permissibility of suspension, i.e., permissibility that knowledge of some rulings depends on ijtihad in sources, and knowledge of only sources is insufficient, along with the absence of impediments like contradiction. I say: you can deny the first entailment (the necessity of knowing all sources for non-partiality) because Amir al-Mu'minin 'Umar (may Allah be pleased with him) and other Companions (may Allah be pleased with them) performed ijtihad in many issues without having the relevant texts present, until those texts were narrated to them and they referred back to them. This is insufficient, for if he is ignorant of the source of some rulings, he cannot derive them, so he is not a mujtahid in all. Knowledge of the source means knowing things by which he can derive the ruling of an incident. And Amir al-Mu'minin even if he did not recall some hadiths, he knew things by which he could derive the ruling of what the report was about, even if by analogy. Indeed, some Companions gave fatwas in some issues, and in some they needed to change, either due to contradiction or similar impediments. Otherwise, it is among the proofs of partial ijtihad as related by the author of al-Kashf from Imam Hujjat al-Islam, so understand. And he argued for the chosen view second: when a person has what pertains to a matter that his attainment depends on, he and someone else who has what pertains to all matters are the same in that matter, as the one can derive it, so can the other. So the statement of that person is accepted and taqlid is forbidden, just as the statement of this person is accepted and taqlid is forbidden. The excellence in other matters is for the other, it has no bearing on that matter. And it was answered by denying equality between the two in deriving that matter, for the one may lack something related to it, so he cannot derive it. If you say: the absolute mujtahid is also not omniscient necessarily, so it is possible that what he does not know has a connection to all matters, so his ijtihad would not be valid. He said: this connection of what he does not know to them is not apparent for the absolute mujtahid; rather, the apparent is no connection. The truth is that raising this probability for the partial mujtahid is even more far-fetched and not heeded by the deniers. They said: everything that the partial mujtahid could be ignorant of may be connected to the ruling, so he cannot derive it, so his ijtihad is not valid. We said: the assumption is that in his opinion he has all that is connected, even if this totality is by the determination of the imams, and the remote probability you mentioned does not harm the assumption, and it is the basis. If you say: the author conceded in response to their second argument the possibility of connection, and here he denies. I say: there it was in response to the argument, and the possibility suffices for denial, but here we need this premise to complete the proof, and the possibility does not suffice. The truth is not hidden from you. I say: and also, if this argument were complete, then every mujtahid would be equal to every other in every area of knowledge, otherwise some would be ignorant of some, and it is possible that it has a bearing on issues, so it would not be valid, but the consequence is false. How? It would require that Ibn Shurayh and Abu Thawr... this and it is forbidden...
Source pages p. 405

Commentary
This passage presents the dispute over whether ijtihad can be partial (tajazzu al-ijtihad). The author sides with the majority that partial ijtihad is permissible, following al-Ghazali and Ibn al-Humam. The text first gives three proofs for the permissibility: (1) leaving knowledge from proof for taqlid is not real knowledge and involves doubt, contrary to the hadith 'leave what brings you doubt'; (2) the hadith 'consult your own heart' indicates preferring one's own ijtihad; (3) a partial mujtahid knows his ruling from proof, so it is obligatory to follow him. Then it examines two arguments used to support the chosen view: first, if ijtihad were only for the complete mujtahid, it would require knowledge of all rulings, which is false; the response denies the entailment. The author rebuts this response by noting that the Companions like 'Umar were not aware of all texts but could still derive rulings. Second, if a partial mujtahid has the relevant knowledge for a specific issue, he is equal to the absolute mujtahid in that issue; the response denies equality due to possible ignorance of connected matters. The author dismisses remote probabilities as not harming the dominant assumption (zann). The text ends with a further argument that if the opposing view were accepted, it would lead to absurd consequences like equality of all mujtahids in all fields.

Key Terms
تجزؤ الاجتهاد (tajazzu al-ijtihad): The permissibility of being a mujtahid in some areas of fiqh without being a mujtahid in all areas
المجتهد المطلق (al-mujtahid al-muthlaq): One who can perform ijtihad in all issues
الظن الغالب (al-zann al-ghalib): Predominant probability; the standard of conviction required for ijtihad

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the primary basis for the Hanafi proof that partial ijtihad is permissible?
A) The consensus of the Companions
B) The hadith 'Leave what brings you doubt'
C) The impossibility of complete ijtihad
D) The statement of Imam al-Ghazali

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits partial ijtihad, allowing a scholar to specialize in one field (like inheritance) and issue binding fatwas, while following taqlid in other areas.

Tajazzi al-Ijtihad: Two Arguments and Rebuttals
Presents two arguments for tajazzi al-ijtihad and their rebuttals

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state and explain the two main arguments for tajazzi al-ijtihad and evaluate the counterarguments using Hanafi usul reasoning.

Core Idea
Ijtihad is divisible – partial expertise suffices for ruling on specific issues.

This passage tackles a foundational usul dispute: can a scholar be a mujtahid in only some areas of fiqh? The Hanafi school argues yes (tajazzi al-ijtihad), while some opponents hold that ijtihad requires comprehensive knowledge. Here, the author presents two proofs for the Hanafi view and then rebuts the opposing counterarguments. Let's work through each step of the debate.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first argument (dalil aqli)
The first argument is rational: if ijtihad were indivisible, a mujtahid would need to know all sources for all rulings, which is impossible. Therefore, ijtihad must be divisible.
2
Analyze the first rebuttal
The opponent denies the second implication (that knowledge of all sources leads to knowledge of all rulings) and argues that a partial mujtahid may suspend judgment when needed.
The author adds that Umar's practice shows partial knowledge sufficed for ijtihad.
3
Identify the second argument (equality in specific issues)
The second argument holds that if someone has all prerequisites for a particular issue, he is equal to a complete mujtahid in that issue, so his ruling is accepted and taqlid is forbidden.
This argument is more subtle, focusing on competency within a limited scope.
4
Analyze the second rebuttal
The opponent denies equality because the partial mujtahid may lack some relevant knowledge that could affect the ruling. The Hanafi reply: this possibility is too remote to undermine the strong presumption (ghalabat al-zann).
The author further argues that the opponent's logic would absurdly equate lesser scholars with the greatest Imams.

واستدل على المختار (أولاً) لو لم يتجز الاجتهاد وانحصر في اجتهاد المجتهد في الكل (لعلم) أي لزم للمجتهد العلم بجميع المآخذ للأحكام كلها (فعلم بجميع الأحكام) وهو باطل قطعاً، فالاجتهاد متجز (وأجيب بمنع الملازمة الثانية لجواز التوقف أي لجواز توقف معرفة بعض الأحكام على الاجتهاد في المآخذ ولا يكفي العلم بالمآخذ فقط وعدم المانع من التعارض وغيره. أقول : ولك أن تمنع الملازمة الأولى (لأن) أمير المؤمنين ( عمر ) رضي الله عنه (وغيره من الصحابة رضي الله تعالى عنهم (اجتهدوا في مسائل كثيرة لم يستحضروا فيها النصوص الواردة (حتى رويت تلك النصوص لهم فرجعوا إليها) وهذا غير وافٍ فإنه إن جهل مأخذ بعض الأحكام فلا يتمكن من استخراجه منها، فلم يكن مجتهداً في الكل، ومعرفة المأخذ عبارة عن معرفة أمور يتمكن بها من استخراج حكم واقعة. وأمير المؤمنين وإن كان لم يستحضر بعض الأحاديث لكنه كان يعلم أموراً يتمكن بها من استخراج حكم ما ورد فيه الخبر ولو بالقياس، نعم : بعض الصحابة أفتوا ببعض المسائل، وفي بعضها احتاجوا إلى الغيرة، فإما للتعارض أو نحوه من الموانع، وإلا فهو من دلائل تجزي الاجتهاد كما حكى صاحب الكشف عن الإمام حجة الإسلام فافهم. (و) استدل على المختار :(ثانياً : إذا حصل ما يتعلق بمسألة مما يتوقف عليه تحصيلها لأحد فهو وغيره ممن حصل له ما يتعلق بالمسائل كلها (فيها) أي في تلك المسألة (سواء) كما أنه يتمكن من استخراجها كذلك يتمكن ذلك، فيقبل قول ذلك ويحرم التقليد، كما يقبل قول هذا التقليد والمزية في غيرها لهذا لا دخل له فيها أي في تلك المسألة (وأجيب بمنع الاستواء بينهما في استخراج تلك المسألة كيف فقد يكون ما لم يعلمه متعلقاً بها فلا يتمكن من استخراجها، فإن قلت : فالمجتهد المطلق أيضاً غير عالم بالجميع بالضرورة فيجوز أن يكون لما لا يعلمه تعلق بجميع المسائل فلا يصح اجتهاد قال : (وهذا) أي التعلق لما لا يعلمه بها (غير ظاهر في المجتهد (المطلق بل الظاهر عدم التعلق. والحق أن إبداء هذا الاحتمال في المجتهد في البعض أيضاً أبعد لا يلتفت إليه المنكرون (قالوا: كل ما يقدر جهله به للمجتهد في البعض (يجوز تعلقه بالحكم فلا يتمكن من استخراجه الحكم، فلا يصح الاجتهاد فيه قلنا): المفروض) فيه حصول جميع ما يتعلق به في ظنه ولو كان هذا الجميع بتقرير الأئمة، والاحتمال (البعيد الذي ذكرتم (لا يقدح في (الظن وعليه المدار، فإن قلت: قد سلم المصنف في جواب استدلالهم الثاني احتمال التعلق وههنا منع . قلت : هناك كان في جواب الاستدلال ويكفي للمنع الاحتمال وههنا الاحتياج إلى هذه المقدمة لإتمام الدليل، ولا يكفيه الاحتمال، والحق غير خاف عليك أقول : وأيضاً لو تم) هذا الدليل (لكان كل مجتهد مساوياً لكل في كل باب في العلم، وإلا لكان البعض مجهولاً للبعض، ويجوز أن يكون له دخل في المسائل فلا يصح واللازم) (باطل وكيف ويلزم أن يكون ابن شريح وأبو ثور مساويين للأئمة الأربعة بل للخلفاء الراشدين والعبادلة وأي خلف أشنع من هذا.
And he argued for the chosen position: (First) If ijtihad were not divisible and were confined to the ijtihad of a complete mujtahid, then it would require knowledge of all sources for all rulings, which is impossible, so ijtihad is divisible. (Answer) Denying the second implication because it is permissible to suspend judgment; knowledge of some rulings depends on ijtihad in sources, and knowledge of sources alone is insufficient. I say: You may deny the first implication because Umar and other Companions performed ijtihad in many cases without recalling the relevant texts; when the texts were later narrated, they referred to them. This is insufficient because if one is ignorant of the source of some rulings, he cannot derive them, so he is not a complete mujtahid. But Umar knew principles enabling derivation even without recalling the text, by analogy. Some Companions gave rulings in some cases and needed change in others due to conflict or obstacles, and that is evidence for divisibility. (Second) If one has obtained what is necessary for a particular issue, he and another who has obtained all necessary things for all issues are equal in that issue; both can derive the ruling, so his opinion is accepted and taqlid is forbidden for him. (Answer) Denying equality, because what he does not know may be related to that issue, so he cannot derive it. It is said: The absolute mujtahid also does not know everything; what he does not know might relate to all issues. He said: This relation is not apparent for the absolute mujtahid; rather, non-relation is apparent. The truth is that this possibility is even more remote for the partial mujtahid and is not considered. (They said: Whatever ignorance is conceivable for the partial mujtahid may be related to the ruling, so he cannot derive it. We said: It is assumed that he has all that relates to it in his estimation, even if this totality is by the determination of the Imams, and the remote possibility you mentioned does not undermine the presumption upon which reliance is placed. If you say: The author conceded in his answer to their second proof the possibility of relation, but here he denies. I say: There, in answer to the proof, possibility suffices for denial; here, this premise is needed to complete the proof, and possibility does not suffice. I say: Also, if this proof were valid, then every mujtahid would be equal in knowledge in every field; otherwise some would be ignorant of some matters, and it might be that these matters have a bearing on rulings, rendering ijtihad invalid. This is absurd, implying that Ibn Shurayh and Abu Thawr would be equal to the four Imams, even to the Rightly Guided Caliphs and the four Abdullahs—what a more repugnant consequence than this!)
Source pages p. 406

Commentary
This passage is a usul dispute on the divisibility of ijtihad (tajazzi al-ijtihad). The author presents two arguments for the chosen position that partial ijtihad is valid. The first argument is rational: if ijtihad were indivisible, a mujtahid would need to know all sources for all rulings, which is impossible; therefore ijtihad is divisible. The rebuttal denies the implication, noting that knowledge of sources alone is insufficient without the ability to apply them, and that a mujtahid can suspend judgment when needed. The author further supports the first argument by citing the example of Umar and other Companions who gave rulings without recalling specific texts, later referring to them when narrated. This shows that they possessed the capability to derive rulings even without complete source knowledge, affirming divisibility. The second argument posits that if someone has acquired all prerequisites for a particular issue, he is equal in that issue to a complete mujtahid, so his ruling is accepted and taqlid is prohibited for him. The rebuttal denies equality because the partial mujtahid may lack some relevant knowledge. The discussion then addresses the absolute mujtahid's similar limitation, but argues that the possibility of unknown relevant knowledge is more remote for the absolute mujtahid, and for the partial mujtahid it is even more remote and not considered. The author concludes that accepting the opponents' logic would lead to absurd consequences, equating lesser scholars with the greatest Imams and Caliphs.

Key Terms
تجزي الاجتهاد (tajazzi al-ijtihad): The idea that ijtihad is divisible; a person can be qualified to perform ijtihad in some areas without being a complete mujtahid in all.
مجتهد مطلق (mujtahid mutlaq): A fully qualified mujtahid who can derive rulings in all areas of fiqh.
مجتهد في البعض (mujtahid fi al-ba'd): A partial mujtahid qualified to derive rulings only in certain fields or issues.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Abu Bakr's consensus on apostasy
D) Ibn Abbas' tafsir of the Quran

Key Takeaway
Ijtihad is divisible: a scholar with sufficient knowledge in a specific area can be a mujtahid in that area, and his ruling is valid and taqlid is prohibited for him there.

Can Prophets Err in Ijtihad? – The Hanafi View
Discussing whether the Prophet and other prophets can err in their ijtihad with the Badr example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi position on prophetic error in ijtihad and evaluate the evidence from the Badr incident.

Core Idea
The majority of Ahl al-Sunna hold that prophets can err in ijtihad, but their errors are corrected and not confirmed.

This lesson examines a key usul al-fiqh dispute: whether prophets can err in their ijtihad. The Hanafi school, with the majority of Ahl al-Sunna, affirms the possibility of error, citing the incident of the prisoners of Badr where the Prophet was reproached after choosing ransom. However, an important nuance is that such errors are not left uncorrected; they are divinely addressed. We will analyze the evidence and the subtle interpretation of Qadi Abu Zaid.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Central Dispute
Recognize that the passage addresses whether prophets (including the Prophet Muhammad) can make mistakes in their ijtihad. Note the two positions: the majority (including Hanafis) says yes; a minority (some Ahl al-Sunna and Rawafidh) says no.
The dispute is about ijtihad, not about conveying revelation—prophets are infallible in that.
2
Examine the Hanafi Evidence
Focus on the Badr incident as the primary dalil. The Prophet chose to ransom prisoners instead of executing them, and the reproach in Quran 8:69 indicates that choice was an error in ijtihad.
The text says 'was an error because of the reproach that came down.'
3
Understand the Concept of 'Not Confirmed'
Even if the Prophet erred, the ruling (ransom) was not overturned because a ruling based on ijtihad is not annulled. This shows that prophets are not left in error—they are corrected but the practical ruling may stand.
Key phrase: 'As for its not being overturned, it is because the ruling of ijtihad cannot be overturned.'
4
Analyze the Counter-Argument by Qadi Abu Zaid
Qadi Abu Zaid offers a subtle interpretation: The ransom was not an error; it was a permissible license (rukhsah). The reproach was for choosing the license over the more excellent resolve (`azimah). This nuance shows the depth of Hanafi legal reasoning.
This interpretation suggests the Prophet did not err but was reproached for choosing a lesser good.
5
Derive the Usul Principle
From this dispute, we extract the principle that prophets are infallible in delivering revelation but may err in ijtihad, though they are divinely guided and corrected. This has implications for the authority of prophetic ijtihad and the status of their rulings.
This principle affects how we understand the Sunnah that is based on ijtihad.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
خاتمة في الاجتهاد والتقليد (فرع: هل يجوز (عليه صلى الله عليه وعلى آله وأصحابه وسلم (الخطأ) في اجتهاده، وكذا في اجتهاد سائر الأنبياء (فالأكثر) من أهل السنة قالوا : (نعم) يجوز (وقيل: لا يجوز ونقل هذا النفي عن الروافض أيضاً (وأما إنه لا يقرر عليه فاتفاق لنا مفاداة أسارى بدر) كان بالرأي وكان خطأ لنزول العتاب ( كما مر ) وأما عدم نقضه فلأن حكم الاجتهاد لا ينقض . روي أن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم تحرج عن أخذ ما فدوا به فلما نزل: فَكُلُوا مِمَّا غَنِمْتُمْ حَلَالًا طَيِّباً ﴾ [الأنفال: ٦٩] أخذ قال القاضي الإمام أبو زيد رحمه الله تعالى : إن هذا لم يكن خطأ كيف وقد قرر عليه، والخطأ مما لا يقرر عليه بل كان أخذ الفداء جائزاً إلا أن قتلهم كان عزيمة والمفاداة رخصة فعوتب باختيار الرخصة، وهذا لا يكاد يفقهه وأيضاً (
Conclusion on Ijtihad and Taqlid (Branch: Is it permissible for him (the Prophet) to err in his ijtihad, and likewise in the ijtihad of other prophets? The majority of Ahl al-Sunna said: Yes, it is permissible. It is said: It is not permissible, and this negation is also narrated from the Rawafidh. As for that he is not confirmed in error, we agree. The ransom of the prisoners of Badr was by opinion and was an error because of the reproach that came down (as has passed). As for its not being overturned, it is because the ruling of ijtihad cannot be overturned. It is narrated that the Prophet hesitated to take the ransom, but when the verse 'So consume what you have taken of war booty as lawful and good' was revealed, he took it. Qadi Imam Abu Zaid said: This was not an error, for how could it be an error when it was confirmed? Error is not confirmed. Rather, taking ransom was permissible, but killing them was a resolve and ransom was a license; he was reproached for choosing the license. This is hardly understood. And also (

Commentary
This passage, part of the conclusion on ijtihad and taqlid, addresses the issue of prophetic error. The majority of Ahl al-Sunna hold that prophets can err in their ijtihad, though they are not left in error but are corrected. Some, including the Rawafidh, deny it. The text uses the incident of the prisoners of Badr, where the Prophet chose ransom (a license) over execution (a resolve), and was reproached for it, indicating it was an error in ijtihad. However, the ruling was not revoked because ijtihad rulings are not annulled. The author adds Qadi Abu Zaid's opinion that the ransom was not actually an error but a permissible choice; the reproach was for opting for the license. This nuance is subtle.

Key Terms
الخطأ في الاجتهاد (al-khata' fi al-ijtihad): Error in deriving a ruling through ijtihad.
المفاداة (al-mufadāh): Ransom, specifically the ransom of prisoners of Badr.

Key Takeaway

Ijtihad in the Prophet's Era: Permission and Precedent
Usul rule on ijtihad during the Prophet's era

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on the permissibility of ijtihad during the Prophet’s lifetime and identify the key evidence and distinctions.

Core Idea
Ijtihad during the Prophet’s era was permitted only in his absence or with his permission, never as a default right.

This passage from Fawatih al-Rahamut addresses a foundational usul question: could the Companions exercise ijtihad while the Prophet was alive? The author surveys multiple opinions and provides key evidences, including the hadith of Mu‘adh and the Banu Qurayza incident. Understanding this issue clarifies the nature of ijtihad and its limits.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core issue
The central question: was ijtihad by others permissible while the Prophet was alive?
2
List the opinions
The text presents several positions: permissibility absolutely, permissibility only in absence, permissibility with permission, and denial of occurrence.
3
Examine the evidence
Note the dalil for each view: Mu'adh hadith, Banu Qurayza incident, arbitration of Sa'd.
4
Understand the Hanafi preference
The preferred view restricts ijtihad to cases of necessity or explicit permission.
5
Extract the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: whether the Companions' ijtihad serves as a valid precedent (sunnah).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
خاتمة في الاجتهاد والتقليد
مسألة: قالت طائفة: لا يجوز اجتهاد غيره في عصره عليه وعلى آله وأصحابه الصلاة والسلام ومختار الأكثر الجواز مطلقاً غيبة وحضوراً وقيل الجواز بشرط غيبته للقضاء لا لغيره بقصة معاذ بن جبل رضي الله عنه وقيل بالإذن يجوز وإذا جاز ففي الوقوع مذاهب الأول نعم واقع مطلقاً حضرة وغيبة لكن ظناً قال السبكي: لم يقل أحد أنه وقع قطعاً. كذا في الحاشية واختاره الآمدي وابن الحاجب والثاني لا يقع وعليه الجبائي وابنه من المعتزلة على المشهور والثالث نعم وقع في الغائب بقصة معاذ بن جبل رحمه الله تعالى، وقد مر، ولأنه قال حين توجه إلى بني قريظة: لا يصلين أحد العصر إلا في بني قريظة فأدرك بعضهم العصر في الطريق، فقال بعضهم لا نصلي حتى نأتيها، وقال بعضهم بل نصلي لم يرد منا ذلك، فذكر ذلك للنبي صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم، فلم يعنف واحداً. رواه البخاري عن ابن عمر وفي رواية محمد بن إسحاق فأتى رجال من بعد العشاء الأخيرة، ولم يصلوا العصر لقول رسول الله صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم: لا يصلين أحد إلا في بني قريظة فشغلهم أمر لم يكن منه بد وأبوا أن يصلوا لقول رسول الله صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم حتى أتوا بني قريظة فصلوا العصر بها بعد العشاء الأخيرة، فما عابهم الله بذلك في كتابه ولا عنفهم به رسول الله صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم وقال: حدث بهذا الحديث أبو إسحق بن يسار عن معبد بن كعب بن مالك الأنصاري دون الحاضر الذي يمكن له السؤال منه صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم وعليه الأكثر، والرابع الوقف مطلقاً حضرة وغيبة وقيل الوقف إلا فيمن غاب وعليه عبد الجبار المعتزلي وكثير الظاهر أنه تفسير للقول بالوقف والحق أن ترك اليقين إلى محتمل الخطأ مختاراً مما يأباه العقل فلا يعتبر بالقياس والاجتهاد عند إمكان السؤال ومن ثمة كانوا يرجعون إليه صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم إلا لضرورة مانعة عن السؤال كالغائب البعيد فإنه لا يقدر على السؤال قبل فوت الحادثة أو للإذن من الرسول صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم بالحكم فإن الرغبة عما أذن به إلى غيره حرام، لأن الإصابة حينئذ مقطوعة كتحكيم سعد بن معاذ في بني قريظة حين حاصرهم رسول الله صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم ونزلوا على حكم سعد بن معاذ فحكم بقتلهم وسبى ذراريهم فقال عليه وعلى آله وأصحابه الصلاة
Conclusion on Ijtihad and Taqlid. Issue: A group said: Ijtihad of others is not permissible in his era (peace be upon him). The choice of the majority is permissibility absolutely, in absence and presence. And it was said: permissibility conditioned on his absence for judgment, not otherwise, based on the story of Mu'adh ibn Jabal. And it was said: with permission it is permissible. And if permissible, then concerning occurrence there are schools. First: Yes, it occurred absolutely, in presence and absence, but based on supposition. Subki said: No one said it occurred definitively. So in the Hashiyah. And it was chosen by Amidi and Ibn Hajib. Second: It did not occur, held by Juba'i and his son of the Mutazila according to the well-known. Third: Yes, it occurred in the absent, based on the story of Mu'adh ibn Jabal, which has passed, and because he said when heading to Banu Qurayza: 'Let no one pray Asr except in Banu Qurayza.' Some of them reached Asr on the way. Some said: We will not pray until we arrive. Some said: Rather we pray; he did not intend that from us. They mentioned that to the Prophet (peace be upon him), and he did not rebuke anyone. Bukhari narrated from Ibn Umar. And in the narration of Muhammad ibn Ishaq: Some men came after Isha prayer, and they had not prayed Asr due to the Prophet's statement: 'Let no one pray except in Banu Qurayza.' They were occupied with something unavoidable and refused to pray until they arrived at Banu Qurayza and prayed Asr there after Isha. Allah did not blame them in His Book nor did the Prophet rebuke them. And he said: This hadith was narrated by Abu Ishaq ibn Yasar from Ma'bad ibn Ka'b ibn Malik al-Ansari. Except for the one present who could ask him (the Prophet). And upon this is the majority. Fourth: Suspension absolutely, in presence and absence. And it was said: suspension except for the absent, held by Abd al-Jabbar al-Mutazili and many. Apparently it is an interpretation of the suspension position. The truth is that abandoning certainty for a possible error willingly is something the intellect rejects, so analogy and ijtihad are not considered when questioning is possible. Therefore, they used to refer to him (peace be upon him) except for a necessity preventing questioning, like the distant absentee who cannot ask before the incident passes, or when the Prophet gave permission to rule, for desiring something else over what he permitted is forbidden, because correctness is then guaranteed, like the arbitration of Sa'd ibn Mu'adh in Banu Qurayza when the Prophet besieged them and they submitted to his judgment, so he ruled to kill them and enslave their children. He (peace be upon him) said...
Source pages p. 413

Commentary
This passage (from the 'Conclusion on Ijtihad and Taqlid') discusses a fundamental usul issue: whether the Companions could exercise ijtihad while the Prophet was alive. The author presents multiple opinions: the majority allowed it absolutely; others conditioned it on the Prophet's absence or his permission; some denied it occurred. Evidence includes the story of Mu'adh ibn Jabal (the Prophet approved his ijtihad in Yemen) and the incident of Banu Qurayza where some Companions interpreted the Prophet's order literally and prayed after the time, and neither Allah nor the Prophet condemned them. The preferred view (al-haqq) is that ijtihad is not permissible when asking the Prophet is possible, except in cases of necessity (e.g., distant traveler) or when the Prophet explicitly permitted. The arbitration of Sa'd ibn Mu'adh is cited as a case where the Prophet authorized ijtihad. The passage establishes that ijtihad during the Prophet's era was exceptional and not the norm.

Key Terms
اجتهاد (ijtihad): Exertion of mental effort to derive a legal ruling from the sources
تقليد (taqlid): Following the ruling of a mujtahid without knowing the evidence

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the preferred view (al-haqq) in the passage, when is ijtihad permissible during the Prophet's lifetime?
A) At all times, whether the Prophet is present or absent
B) Only when the Prophet is absent or gives permission, not when asking is possible
C) Never, because certainty is required
D) Only for matters not addressed by revelation

Key Takeaway
Ijtihad during the Prophet's era was not the norm; it required either his absence, permission, or a necessity that prevented direct questioning.

Ijtihad — Independent Reasoning
