ولي وهو يرى انعقاد النكاح من غير ولي (ثم) مجتهد (آخر بولي) وهو يرى اشتراط الولي، فالنكاح الأوّل نافذ عند الأول والثاني باطل، وعند الثاني الأول فاسد والثاني صحيح، وكلاهما حقان في نفس الأمر، فتكون امرأة واحدة حلالاً لزوجين (وأجيب بأنه مشترك الإلزام) علينا وعليكم (إذ لا خلاف بيننا وبينكم (في وجوب اتباع (الظن فيكون اتباع الزوجة والزوج ظنيهما واجباً وظن أحدهما الحرمة والآخر الحل . وكذا يجب على المتزوجين اتباع ظنيهما وظن كل منهما الحل له وما قيل لا يلزم إلا الحل عند مجتهد والحرمة عند آخر، ففيه أن الوطء فعل واحد لا يتم إلا بهما، فحينئذ يلزم اتصاف فعل واحد بهما (والحل) للدليل (أن مثله كتعارض دليلين فلا حكم أي لا يحكم بحكم بل يرفع إلى حاكم ، فما حكم به فهو الحكم) وهذا كله غير وافٍ فإنّ هذا الحل يكفي لدفع النقض لا لدفع الدليل، لأن وجوب العمل بالاجتهاد إنما هو إذا لم يمنع مانع وههنا تعارض الاجتهادين مانع فيرفع إلى حاكم فبقضائه
A conclusion on ijtihad, taqlid, and knowledge. It is entailing the conjunction of opposites, and objections are raised against it. First: The object of zann is not God's ruling, i.e., it is not that it is God's ruling, but what is more fitting to the principles. Qat' relates to its being God's ruling. The two objects differ. But it is impossible for any of God's rulings to be unfitting to the principles; so its being more fitting is a necessary concomitant of its being God's ruling. Zann of it necessitates the possibility of its non-existence; this possibility exists alongside qat' of its being God's ruling, which is like the conjunction of opposites. Ponder. Second: The mujtahid according to them is like a prophet of a dispensation regarding the ruling. As the prophet rules by his revelation, so he rules by his zann. Zann, like revelation, necessarily causes the ruling unless reversal (similar to abrogation) is established. So zann does not remain when qat' exists; only qat' remains. Zann is like a preparatory cause. The possibility of reversal does not harm qat', as the possibility of abrogation does not harm it. But it is agreed that what is certain does not admit ijtihad leading to its opposite. If certainty occurs here, reversal would not be valid. Reversal is like abrogation only if the ruling remains conjectural. Unless one distinguishes between certainty attained through zann and certainty from a definitive proof that directly causes certainty; the first can be overturned by ijtihad, not the second. With this answer is refuted what was said: If zann causes knowledge, then zann of its opposite while remembering the cause of knowledge would be impossible, because knowledge is necessary for its cause unlike an indication which has no rational connection. The refutation is that reversal is from knowledge to another knowledge concerning its opposite, like reversal from knowledge of the abrogated to knowledge of the abrogator. The secret is that what is attained after zann is knowledge that does not admit contradiction from the start, but admits elevation by a later lifting; its continuance is conjectural, so it may be replaced by a substitute which is zann of its opposite. Third: It is a common objection: There is consensus between us and you on the obligation to follow zann with certainty. This is also a conjunction. The response: The locus of zann for us is His ruling, and the locus of knowledge is the prohibition of opposing what his opinion leads to as long as he is upon his zann; thus the two objects differ, no impossibility. If it is said: With this answer you give, they can respond to your proof by saying the object of zann is that the proof is a proof, and the object of knowledge is the establishedness of its indication which is God's ruling as long as it is a proof; so the two objects also differ. The answer is as in al-Mukhtasar: That it is a proof is a legal ruling even if not practical. So if he zanns it as a proof, he knows it; otherwise he does not know it but rather doubts whether it is actually a proof, in which case worship by another is permissible, so the indicated ruling no longer remains certain. Thus knowledge that the proof is a proof is necessary, while it was assumed conjectural; this is circular. But they based knowledge of the indicated on zann of the ruling only, making it like a preparation, not claiming knowledge of the proof. The permissibility of worship by another as long as it is conjectural is denied; worship is only by what is conjectured. Permissibility only arises after the invalidity of his zann, no impossibility. Then it's invalidated by its indication like the arrival of the abrogator, though the proof's being a proof is rational, not legal. Obligation to act according to it is legal. This does not harm al-Mukhtasar. The most appropriate answer: Zann in the proof necessitates zann in the indicated, since no knowledge of the indicated exists with possibility in the proof. This is solid, but the disputant may argue that certainty was not attained through the proof, only zann of the indicated, then it led to certainty of the opposite; there is no impossibility except in attaining certainty of the indicated with the proof's conjecturality. Ponder. Second proof for the chosen view: If the two proofs of conflicting ijtihads are equal, they cancel out; then judging truth is arbitrary; otherwise the preponderant is correct, so no truth for each. The answer: Preponderance according to them follows the mujtahid's zann. Both are preponderant in the mujtahids' zann, so their indications are conjectural for them, so both are truth for them in reality. I say: Error in preponderance does not necessitate error in the ruling; we do not concede that the correct is the preponderant. Third proof: Consensus on the legitimacy of debate between mujtahids; its benefit is the emergence of truth. If no mujtahid could err, this consensus-based practice would have no benefit. The answer: The benefit is not restricted; it may be to clarify preponderance (so they return to one proof and one ruling) or equality (so they seek another proof). I say: After they know that both are God's ruling through their ijtihad, engaging in this is superfluous. Fourth proof: On taswib, if a mujtahid husband says to his wife 'You are divorced bain' then 'I take you back' while he sees permissibility, and the wife sees prohibition, both are truth; the act of intercourse becomes both permissible and forbidden. Also, if a woman marries one mujtahid without a guardian (he deems it valid) and another with a guardian (he deems it valid), both marriages are valid in reality, so one woman is lawful to two husbands. The answer: This is a common objection; there is no difference between us and you in the obligation to follow zann. The solution is like conflicting proofs: no ruling is given, but the matter is raised to a judge; what he rules is the ruling. This is insufficient; it only refutes the objection, not the proof.
Source pages p. 419

Commentary
This passage is a defense of the view that every mujtahid is correct (taswib) in his ijtihad. The author addresses several objections raised against this view. The first objection is that if zann (conjecture) leads to certainty about God's ruling, then the possibility of the opposite being true would be impossible, but that possibility coexists with certainty, which is a contradiction. The author responds by distinguishing the objects: zann is about what is most fitting to the principles, while qat' is about its being God's ruling; these are different. The second objection analogizes the mujtahid to a prophet: just as a prophet's revelation leads to a ruling, so does the mujtahid's zann. However, if zann leads to certainty, then the mujtahid should not be able to change his ruling, yet he can through further ijtihad. The author explains that the certainty attained through zann is not absolute; it can be overturned by a new ijtihad, like abrogation. The third objection claims that consensus requires following zann with certainty, leading to the same problem. The author says the object of zann is the ruling itself, while the object of knowledge is the prohibition of opposing it; thus no conflict. A fourth argument from opponents is raised and answered with a distinction between the role of the proof. The author then provides three proofs for taswib: (1) if two proofs are equal, they cancel out, but in reality each mujtahid follows his preponderant zann; (2) the consensus on debate does not necessarily aim at finding the single truth but may aim at clarifying preponderance or equality; (3) practical examples like a husband and wife having contradictory rulings on divorce or a woman married to two mujtahids with different views. The author refutes these by saying that in such cases the matter is to be resolved by a judge. Overall, the passage asserts that ijitihad is valid for every mujtahid and multiple contradictory rulings can all be correct in the sense that each mujtahid is required to follow his own zann.

Key Terms
الظن (al-zann): Conjecture or probability that is preponderant but not certain; the basis of ijtihad.
القطع (al-qat'): Certain knowledge that admits no possibility of error.
المجتهد (al-mujtahid): One who performs ijtihad to derive rulings from the sources.
التصويب (al-taswib): The view that every mujtahid is correct in his ijtihad.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi response to the first objection, what is the object of zann?
A) The ruling being God's ruling
B) What is most fitting to the principles
C) The certainty of the ruling
D) The impossibility of the opposite

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi defense of taswib relies on distinguishing the objects of zann and qat' to resolve the apparent contradiction of certainty coexisting with possibility of error.

Ijtihad — Independent Reasoning
advanced
12 min
Proofs for Taswib and the Resolution of Practical Conflicts
Providing three proofs for taswib and addressing practical conflicts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three proofs for taswib and explain how the Hanafi school resolves practical conflicts arising from contradictory ijtihads.

Core Idea
Taswib is supported by three proofs; practical conflicts are resolved by referring to a judge, not by negating one ijtihad.

After addressing conceptual objections, the passage provides three positive proofs for taswib and then tackles practical scenarios where contradictory ijtihads create apparent dilemmas. These proofs include: (1) equal proofs cancel out, but each mujtahid follows his preponderant zann; (2) the benefit of debate is clarifying preponderance or equality, not necessarily finding a single truth; (3) practical examples of contradictory rulings on divorce and marriage. The Hanafi solution for when two valid ijtihads conflict is to refer the matter to a judge, whose ruling becomes the operative law in that case.

Guided Path
1
State the first proof (equal proofs)
Opponent: If two proofs are equal, they cancel out; thus no ruling can be correct. Hanafi: Preponderance follows the mujtahid's zann, so both are correct for their respective mujtahids.
The response emphasizes that error in preponderance does not mean error in the ruling itself.
2
Analyze the second proof (consensus on debate)
Opponent: Debate is prescribed to discover truth; if no one errs, debate is pointless. Hanafi: The benefit could be clarifying preponderance or equality, not necessarily discovering a single truth.
The text adds a nuance: after knowing both are God's ruling through ijtihad, seeking further truth is superfluous.
3
Examine the third proof (practical conflicts)
Opponent: Taswib leads to contradictions like a woman being both permissible and forbidden to her husband. Hanafi: This is a common objection; the solution is to raise the case to a judge whose ruling resolves the conflict.
The passage notes this answer refutes the objection but does not prove taswib.
4
Understand the role of the judge
When two ijtihads conflict, the judge's ruling becomes the decisive one for the case. This prevents practical chaos while preserving the theoretical correctness of each mujtahid's ijtihad.
This is a key mechanism in Hanafi legal theory.

A conclusion on ijtihad, taqlid, and knowledge. It is entailing the conjunction of opposites, and objections are raised against it. First: The object of zann is not God's ruling, i.e., it is not that it is God's ruling, but what is more fitting to the principles. Qat' relates to its being God's ruling. The two objects differ. But it is impossible for any of God's rulings to be unfitting to the principles; so its being more fitting is a necessary concomitant of its being God's ruling. Zann of it necessitates the possibility of its non-existence; this possibility exists alongside qat' of its being God's ruling, which is like the conjunction of opposites. Ponder. Second: The mujtahid according to them is like a prophet of a dispensation regarding the ruling. As the prophet rules by his revelation, so he rules by his zann. Zann, like revelation, necessarily causes the ruling unless reversal (similar to abrogation) is established. So zann does not remain when qat' exists; only qat' remains. Zann is like a preparatory cause. The possibility of reversal does not harm qat', as the possibility of abrogation does not harm it. But it is agreed that what is certain does not admit ijtihad leading to its opposite. If certainty occurs here, reversal would not be valid. Reversal is like abrogation only if the ruling remains conjectural. Unless one distinguishes between certainty attained through zann and certainty from a definitive proof that directly causes certainty; the first can be overturned by ijtihad, not the second. With this answer is refuted what was said: If zann causes knowledge, then zann of its opposite while remembering the cause of knowledge would be impossible, because knowledge is necessary for its cause unlike an indication which has no rational connection. The refutation is that reversal is from knowledge to another knowledge concerning its opposite, like reversal from knowledge of the abrogated to knowledge of the abrogator. The secret is that what is attained after zann is knowledge that does not admit contradiction from the start, but admits elevation by a later lifting; its continuance is conjectural, so it may be replaced by a substitute which is zann of its opposite. Third: It is a common objection: There is consensus between us and you on the obligation to follow zann with certainty. This is also a conjunction. The response: The locus of zann for us is His ruling, and the locus of knowledge is the prohibition of opposing what his opinion leads to as long as he is upon his zann; thus the two objects differ, no impossibility. If it is said: With this answer you give, they can respond to your proof by saying the object of zann is that the proof is a proof, and the object of knowledge is the establishedness of its indication which is God's ruling as long as it is a proof; so the two objects also differ. The answer is as in al-Mukhtasar: That it is a proof is a legal ruling even if not practical. So if he zanns it as a proof, he knows it; otherwise he does not know it but rather doubts whether it is actually a proof, in which case worship by another is permissible, so the indicated ruling no longer remains certain. Thus knowledge that the proof is a proof is necessary, while it was assumed conjectural; this is circular. But they based knowledge of the indicated on zann of the ruling only, making it like a preparation, not claiming knowledge of the proof. The permissibility of worship by another as long as it is conjectural is denied; worship is only by what is conjectured. Permissibility only arises after the invalidity of his zann, no impossibility. Then it's invalidated by its indication like the arrival of the abrogator, though the proof's being a proof is rational, not legal. Obligation to act according to it is legal. This does not harm al-Mukhtasar. The most appropriate answer: Zann in the proof necessitates zann in the indicated, since no knowledge of the indicated exists with possibility in the proof. This is solid, but the disputant may argue that certainty was not attained through the proof, only zann of the indicated, then it led to certainty of the opposite; there is no impossibility except in attaining certainty of the indicated with the proof's conjecturality. Ponder. Second proof for the chosen view: If the two proofs of conflicting ijtihads are equal, they cancel out; then judging truth is arbitrary; otherwise the preponderant is correct, so no truth for each. The answer: Preponderance according to them follows the mujtahid's zann. Both are preponderant in the mujtahids' zann, so their indications are conjectural for them, so both are truth for them in reality. I say: Error in preponderance does not necessitate error in the ruling; we do not concede that the correct is the preponderant. Third proof: Consensus on the legitimacy of debate between mujtahids; its benefit is the emergence of truth. If no mujtahid could err, this consensus-based practice would have no benefit. The answer: The benefit is not restricted; it may be to clarify preponderance (so they return to one proof and one ruling) or equality (so they seek another proof). I say: After they know that both are God's ruling through their ijtihad, engaging in this is superfluous. Fourth proof: On taswib, if a mujtahid husband says to his wife 'You are divorced bain' then 'I take you back' while he sees permissibility, and the wife sees prohibition, both are truth; the act of intercourse becomes both permissible and forbidden. Also, if a woman marries one mujtahid without a guardian (he deems it valid) and another with a guardian (he deems it valid), both marriages are valid in reality, so one woman is lawful to two husbands. The answer: This is a common objection; there is no difference between us and you in the obligation to follow zann. The solution is like conflicting proofs: no ruling is given, but the matter is raised to a judge; what he rules is the ruling. This is insufficient; it only refutes the objection, not the proof.
Source pages p. 419

Commentary
This passage is a defense of the view that every mujtahid is correct (taswib) in his ijtihad. The author addresses several objections raised against this view. The first objection is that if zann (conjecture) leads to certainty about God's ruling, then the possibility of the opposite being true would be impossible, but that possibility coexists with certainty, which is a contradiction. The author responds by distinguishing the objects: zann is about what is most fitting to the principles, while qat' is about its being God's ruling; these are different. The second objection analogizes the mujtahid to a prophet: just as a prophet's revelation leads to a ruling, so does the mujtahid's zann. However, if zann leads to certainty, then the mujtahid should not be able to change his ruling, yet he can through further ijtihad. The author explains that the certainty attained through zann is not absolute; it can be overturned by a new ijtihad, like abrogation. The third objection claims that consensus requires following zann with certainty, leading to the same problem. The author says the object of zann is the ruling itself, while the object of knowledge is the prohibition of opposing it; thus no conflict. A fourth argument from opponents is raised and answered with a distinction between the role of the proof. The author then provides three proofs for taswib: (1) if two proofs are equal, they cancel out, but in reality each mujtahid follows his preponderant zann; (2) the consensus on debate does not necessarily aim at finding the single truth but may aim at clarifying preponderance or equality; (3) practical examples like a husband and wife having contradictory rulings on divorce or a woman married to two mujtahids with different views. The author refutes these by saying that in such cases the matter is to be resolved by a judge. Overall, the passage asserts that ijitihad is valid for every mujtahid and multiple contradictory rulings can all be correct in the sense that each mujtahid is required to follow his own zann.

Key Terms
الظن (al-zann): Conjecture or probability that is preponderant but not certain; the basis of ijtihad.
القطع (al-qat'): Certain knowledge that admits no possibility of error.
المجتهد (al-mujtahid): One who performs ijtihad to derive rulings from the sources.
التصويب (al-taswib): The view that every mujtahid is correct in his ijtihad.
الحاكم (al-hakim): Judge who resolves conflicting ijtihads by issuing a binding ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the Hanafi solution when two valid ijtihads lead to contradictory practical outcomes?
A) The ruling that is more popular is followed.
B) The case is referred to a judge, whose ruling becomes the operative one.
C) Both rulings are followed, leaving the choice to the individual.
D) The later ijtihad abrogates the earlier one.

Key Takeaway
Taswib is supported by three proofs; practical conflicts from contradictory ijtihads are resolved by referring the case to a judge, preserving the theoretical correctness of each mujtahid.

The Ijma' Argument for Taswib and the Hanafi Critique
Arguing from consensus on debate among mujtahids

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi critique of the taswib argument from ijma' by identifying the logical fallacy of tahsil al-hasil (obtaining the already obtained).

Core Idea
If every mujtahid already has God's ruling through ijtihad, debate for tarjih is tahsil al-hasil—obtaining what is already obtained.

This passage presents the third proof for the Musawwibun (those who hold that every mujtahid is correct): there is consensus (ijma') that debate among mujtahids is legitimate in the Shari'ah. They argue that the sole purpose of such debate is to discover truth, implying that error is possible—otherwise debate would be pointless. The Hanafi (mukhatti'un) response is incisive: they deny that the benefit is limited to truth-discovery, suggesting it could be to clarify preponderance (tarjih). However, the annotator rebuts even this: after each mujtahid already knows his ruling as God's ruling for him, seeking tarjih or another proof is redundant (tahsil al-hasil). Thus the ijma' proof actually supports the Hanafi view that only one mujtahid is correct.

Guided Path
1
State the taswib argument
Identify the Musawwibun's claim: ijma' proves debate is legitimate, and the only benefit is discovering truth. Therefore, error must be possible, and only one mujtahid is correct.
This is the third proof mentioned in the text.
2
Identify the rebuttal
The opposing school (Musawwibun) responds by denying that truth-discovery is the only benefit. They propose that the benefit could be to clarify preponderance (tarjih) or to recognize equality, leading them to seek another proof.
This rebuttal attempts to preserve the possibility that all mujtahids are correct.
3
Recognize the Hanafi response
The Hanafi annotator argues that if each mujtahid already knows his ruling is God's ruling for him, then engaging in tarjih or seeking another proof is tahsil al-hasil—a futile repetition. Thus the rebuttal fails.
Tahsil al-hasil is a key critique: you cannot obtain what is already obtained.
4
Understand the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: a student learning from two mujtahids must choose one based on preponderance, not accept both. This aligns with the Hanafi view that only one ruling is correct in the sight of God.
The dispute is not merely theoretical; it affects how one follows conflicting ijtihads.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و) استدل على المختار :(ثالثاً : أجمعوا على شرع المناظرة بين المجتهدين، وإنما فائدتها ظهور الصواب) فلو لم يحتمل كل مجتهد الخطأ لما كان لهذا الشرع المجمع عليه فائدة (وأجيب بمنع الحصر) أي بمنع حصر الفائدة في ظهور الصواب لجواز تبيين (الترجيح أي يجوز أن تكون الفائدة تبيين الترجيح (فيرجعان إلى دليل (واحد) وحكم واحد (أو) تبيين (التساوي فيطلبان دليلاً آخر، أقول: بعد علمهما بأن كليهما حكم (الله باجتهادهما فالاشتغال بها لذلك) الترجيح أو التساوي تحصيل الحاصل، فإنه لا مزيد عليه بعد الرجوع إلى الراجح أو إلى دليل آخر مغاير له، فإنه بعد هذا يحصل أيضاً حكم الله. وقبله كان كذلك، فلا فائدة في شرع المناظرة أصلاً
And (they) argued for the chosen position: Thirdly: There is consensus on the legitimacy of debate between mujtahids, and its only benefit is the emergence of truth. For if every mujtahid were not liable to err, this consensually established practice would have no benefit. And it was answered by denying the exclusivity — i.e., by denying that the only benefit is the emergence of truth, because it is possible that the benefit is clarification of preponderance — so they both refer back to one proof and one ruling, or clarification of equality — so they seek another proof. I say: After they both know that each one's ruling is God's ruling through his ijtihad, then engaging in this (preponderance or equality) is obtaining what is already obtained; for there is nothing additional after reverting to the preponderant or to another different proof, because after this too God's ruling is obtained. And before it was the same, so there is absolutely no benefit in the legitimacy of debate.
Source pages p. 420

Commentary
This passage presents the third proof for taswib: there is consensus (ijma') that debate among mujtahids is legitimate in the Shari'ah, and its only purpose is to discover truth. If every mujtahid were correct, then debate would be pointless because no error needs correction. Therefore, error must be possible, meaning only one is correct. The rebuttal: the benefit of debate is not limited to discovering truth; it could be to clarify which view is preponderant (tarjih) or that they are equal, leading them to seek further evidence. The annotator (I say) objects: if each mujtahid already knows his own view is God's ruling for him, then engaging in debate for tarjih or equality is redundant — he already has his ruling. So even this rebuttal fails, and the proof stands in the Hanafi view.

Key Terms
تحصيل الحاصل (taḥṣīl al-ḥāṣil): Obtaining what is already obtained; a futile action.
ترجيح (tarjīḥ): Clarifying preponderance; determining which of two conflicting proofs or rulings is stronger.
إجماع (ijmāʿ): Consensus of the mujtahids of the ummah on a legal ruling or principle.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Debate is beneficial because it produces new knowledge
B) Tarjih is a new benefit that was not present before
C) After each mujtahid already has his ruling, seeking tarjih is redundant
D) Debate should be banned because it leads to confusion

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi use of tahsil al-hasil to critique the tarjih rebuttal demonstrates that ijma' on debate supports the mukhatti'un position: only one mujtahid is correct, and debate's value lies in discovering truth.

Fourth Proof: Contradictory Rulings on Marriage
Illustrating absurd consequences of taswib in marriage cases

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi argument against taswib using the example of conflicting marriage rulings and evaluate the musawwibun's responses.

Core Idea
Taswib collapses when two mujtahids rule oppositely on one marriage — reality cannot hold both.

This passage presents the fourth Hanafi proof against taswib (that every mujtahid is correct). The author illustrates with two scenarios: a wife who is a mujtahida and her mujtahid husband holding conflicting rulings on divorce, and a woman married to two mujtahids under different conditions. The musawwibun object that each follows his own opinion, but the Hanafi critique shows their answers lead to impossible consequences. The final resolution is to refer conflicting ijtihads to a judge.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proof scenarios
The passage gives two examples: (1) A husband mujtahid divorces his wife then claims to take her back; the wife sees the return as invalid. Both rulings are claimed correct under taswib, so intercourse would be both halal and haram. (2) Two mujtahids marry the same woman: one without a guardian, one with; each marriage is valid in its mujtahid's view, leading to a woman being halal for two men.
These are rational (aqli) proofs showing practical absurdity.
2
Analyze the musawwibun's response
The musawwibun reply that each mujtahid acts on his own opinion, so no real-world contradiction exists—the husband's act is halal for him, haram for the wife. They also say the example is like conflicting evidences (taʿāruḍ al-dalīlayn) where no ruling is given until a judge decides.
This response attempts to relativize the rulings.
3
Evaluate the Hanafi counter-argument
The Hanafi author argues that the musawwibun's answer only wards off an objection (naqd) but does not refute the proof (dalil). The act of intercourse is a single act requiring both partners; if both ijtihads are correct in reality, the act would simultaneously be halal and haram, which is impossible. Referral to a judge is a practical solution but does not prove both ijtihads are correct in the divine law.
The key point: relative permissibility fails because the act itself cannot have two contradictory descriptions.
4
Understand the final Hanafi position
The Hanafi solution: conflicting ijtihads act as a preventer (maniʿ) that lifts the obligation to follow individual ijtihad. The matter is raised to a judge, whose ruling chooses one ijtihad as operative. In contrast, taswib would require both ijtihads to be correct in reality, leading to impossible simultaneity of halal and haram.
The Hanafi school (mukhatti'un) holds that only one mujtahid is correct; the other errs, but is excused.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و) استدل على المختار رابعاً : يلزم على التصويب) لكل مجتهد (حل المجتهدة وحرمتها) معاً على بعلها (لو قال بعلها المجتهد : أنت بائن، ثم قال : راجعتك والرجل يرى الحل) أي حل الرجعة بعد التطليق بهذا النحو والمرأة الحرمة) وكلاهما حق، فيكون فعل المرأة هو الوطء حلالاً وحراماً معاً (و) يلزم عليه أيضاً (حلها ) أي حل المرأة المجتهدة (لاثنين لو تزوجها مجتهد بلا ولي وهو يرى انعقاد النكاح من غير ولي (ثم) مجتهد (آخر بولي) وهو يرى اشتراط الولي، فالنكاح الأوّل نافذ عند الأول والثاني باطل، وعند الثاني الأول فاسد والثاني صحيح، وكلاهما حقان في نفس الأمر، فتكون امرأة واحدة حلالاً لزوجين (وأجيب بأنه مشترك الإلزام) علينا وعليكم (إذ لا خلاف بيننا وبينكم (في وجوب اتباع (الظن فيكون اتباع الزوجة والزوج ظنيهما واجباً وظن أحدهما الحرمة والآخر الحل . وكذا يجب على المتزوجين اتباع ظنيهما وظن كل منهما الحل له وما قيل لا يلزم إلا الحل عند مجتهد والحرمة عند آخر، ففيه أن الوطء فعل واحد لا يتم إلا بهما، فحينئذ يلزم اتصاف فعل واحد بهما (والحل) للدليل (أن مثله كتعارض دليلين فلا حكم أي لا يحكم بحكم بل يرفع إلى حاكم ، فما حكم به فهو الحكم) وهذا كله غير وافٍ فإنّ هذا الحل يكفي لدفع النقض لا لدفع الدليل، لأن وجوب العمل بالاجتهاد إنما هو إذا لم يمنع مانع وههنا تعارض الاجتهادين مانع فيرفع إلى حاكم فبقضائه يترجح أحد الاجتهادين فيعمل. وأما عند هؤلاء فاجتهاد كل مطابق للواقع فيجتمع الحل والحرمة، أو الحل لاثنين في زمان واحد قطعاً، بخلاف ما نحن فيه. فإنّ أحد الاجتهادين خطأ في الواقع، وإنما كان لنا العمل بكل انفراداً، وإذا اجتمعا فبترجيح آخر فافهم. (وأما الجواب بأن الحل إنما هو بالإضافة إلى أحدهما والحرمة بالإضافة إلى الآخر فلا استحالة فيه (كما في الشرح، فأقول : لا يخفى وهنه، لأن ذلك : أي حل المرأة والحرمة متعاكس) فعند أحدهما الفعل له فيها حلال وللآخر حرام، وعند الآخر بالعكس، والمفروض أن كليهما صوابان مطابقان للواقع فيجتمع الحل لهما في زمان واحد في الصورة الثانية (تدبر)
And (they) argued for the chosen position fourthly: It follows from taswib (that every mujtahid is correct) that a woman (who is a mujtahidah) is both halal and haram simultaneously for her husband. If her husband (a mujtahid) says: 'You are bā'in (divorced irrevocably),' then says: 'I take you back,' while the husband sees it as permissible (i.e., he believes divorce allows taking back in this way) and the woman sees it as forbidden, and both are correct, then the act of intercourse would be both halal and haram simultaneously. Also, it follows that a woman is permissible to two men: if a mujtahid marries her without a guardian, believing marriage is valid without a guardian; then another mujtahid marries her with a guardian, believing a guardian is necessary. The first marriage is valid according to the first, invalid according to the second; the first is invalid and the second valid according to the second, and both are true in reality, so one woman becomes permissible to two husbands. It was answered that this is a shared objection (against us and you), because there is no dispute among us that following opinion is obligatory. So the wife and husband must follow their respective opinions; the husband's opinion gives permissibility, the wife's gives prohibition. Similarly, the two men must follow their opinions, each believing the woman is permissible to him. And it is said that only permissibility for one mujtahid and prohibition for another follow, but this is not so because the act of intercourse is one act that cannot happen without both; thus it would be described as both. (The solution) for the proof: that its example is like conflicting evidences, so no ruling is given; rather it is referred to a judge, and what he judges is the ruling. But all this is insufficient, because this solution wards off the objection (naqd) but not the proof (dalil). For obligation to act on ijtihad is only when there is no preventing factor; and here the conflicting ijtihads are a preventing factor, so it is referred to a judge, and by his judgment one of the ijtihads prevails and is acted upon. But according to these (musawwibun), each ijtihad corresponds to reality, so halal and haram, or permissibility to two men, would coincide in one time definitely, unlike our position (mukhatti'un). For in our view, one of the two ijtihads is erroneous in reality, but we act on each individually; when they conflict, we resort to another preference. Understand. (As for the answer that permissibility is relative to one and prohibition relative to the other, there is no impossibility) as in the sharh. I say: its weakness is clear, because that — i.e., the woman's permissibility and prohibition — are reversed: according to one, the act is halal for him and haram for the other, and according to the other it is the opposite, while the assumption is that both are correct and correspond to reality, so permissibility for both at one time occurs in the second scenario. Reflect.
Source pages p. 420

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth proof against taswib by showing absurd consequences. The Hanafi position (mukhatti'un) argues that if every mujtahid is correct, contradictory rulings about a woman's status would be simultaneously true: she could be both divorced and not divorced depending on the spouses' ijtihads, or married to two men simultaneously. The rebuttal suggests that each mujtahid acts on his own opinion, so there is no actual contradiction in the real world — each acts according to his own ruling. The Hanafi refines this: the problem arises when the same act (e.g., intercourse) requires agreement between two people with conflicting rulings; at that point, the act would have two contradictory descriptions. The final Hanafi solution: when ijtihads conflict, the matter is referred to a judge, who chooses one ruling. This avoids the absurdity. The annotator further critiques the musawwibun's position: if both ijtihads correspond to reality, then permissibility and prohibition would truly coexist for the same act, which is impossible — unlike the Hanafi view where only one is real and the other is error.

Key Terms
المجتهدة (al-mujtahida): A woman who is capable of ijtihad; used in the example to show conflicting rulings from her and her husband.
تعارض الاجتهادين (taʿāruḍ al-ijtihādayn): Conflict between two ijtihads; the solution is to refer to a judge.

Ijtihad — Independent Reasoning

Musawwibun's First Proof and Hanafi Response
Logical argument and rebuttal on ijtihad correctness

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi response to the Musawwibun's first proof and apply the principle that obligation is based on accessible opinion, not hidden truth.

Core Idea
A mistaken mujtahid must act on his ijtihad; truth is not obligatory until known.

In this passage, we encounter one of the classic arguments of the Musawwibun (those who hold every mujtahid is correct) against the Mukhatti'un (those who hold only one is correct). The argument presents a dilemma: if only one mujtahid is correct, the mistaken one is either obligated to follow the truth (impossible) or obligated to follow error (contradictory). The Hanafi school answers by choosing the latter horn and showing that acting on one's best effort is not only permissible but obligatory, supported by the example of a hidden definitive text. This lesson walks through the logical structure of the dispute and the Hanafi resolution, which hinges on the mercy of the Shari'ah and the limitation of obligation to what is accessible.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Musawwibun's dilemma
The Musawwibun argue: if only one mujtahid's opinion is correct, then the mistaken mujtahid faces a contradiction. Either he is obligated to follow the truth (which he does not know) or he is obligated to follow his error (which is wrong). Both options seem unacceptable.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum argument.
2
Analyze the Hanafi response: choosing the second horn
The Hanafis respond by explicitly choosing the second alternative: the mistaken mujtahid is obligated to act on his ijtihad, even if it is erroneous. This is not contradictory because the Shari'ah sometimes commands action based on opinion (zann) rather than certain truth.
The Hanafi position is that obligation is tied to what one can reach through ijtihad, not to the hidden reality.
3
Understand the supporting example: the hidden definitive text
The Hanafis cite the case where a definitive text (qati') is unknown to the mujtahid. He makes ijtihad contrary to it and must act on his ijtihad until the text becomes apparent. This is agreed upon by consensus. This shows that acting on error is sometimes obligatory.
This example demonstrates that the Shari'ah itself validates acting on opinion even when it diverges from the actual ruling.
4
Grasp the underlying principle: mercy and sincerity
The secret (sirr) behind this ruling is that Allah's command is based on mercy. He has restricted obligation to what one can achieve through opinion, focusing on sincerity and obedience of the heart rather than infallible correctness.
This is a key Hanafi usul principle: taklif is based on capacity (wus').
5
Apply the principle to a new scenario
Consider a mujtahid who derives a ruling on a new financial transaction. According to the Hanafi view, if his ijtihad is later shown to conflict with a hidden definitive text, he is not sinful for his earlier ruling; he is rewarded for his effort, and his ruling was binding on those who followed it until the truth became clear.
This illustrates the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence) of the dispute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المصوّبون (قالوا أولاً : لو كان المصيب (واحداً من المجتهدين المختلفين (وجب النقيضان على المخطىء إن وجب الصواب عليه أيضاً كما وجب ما أدى إليه اجتهاده ( وإلا يجب الصواب (وجب العمل بالخطأ وحرم بالصواب) وهو خلاف المعقول والأظهر أن يقال: إن وجب على الخاطيء العمل بالصواب فهو تكليف بما لا وسع له فيه وبما لا علم له به، وإلا وجب العمل بالخطأ وحرم بالصواب (وأجيب باختيار) الشق الثاني ومنع بطلان (التالي) وهو وجوب العمل بالخطأ كما فيما لو خفي عليه (قاطع موجود واجتهد بخلافه وجب العمل به إلى ظهوره مع أنه خطأ (اتفاقاً) والسر فيه أن الأمر برحمته الأزلية سهل وهي قصر العمل على الظن مطابقاً أو غير مطابق، ومطمح نظره إلى الإخلاص والإطاعة بالقلب فافهم.
The musawwibun said: First: If the correct one is only one of the differing mujtahids, then two contradictories would be incumbent on the mistaken one if correctness were also obligatory upon him as his ijtihad led him to believe; otherwise, if correctness is not obligatory, then acting on error would be obligatory and acting on truth forbidden — which is contrary to reason. The more apparent is to say: If acting on truth is obligatory upon the mistaken, that is a burden beyond his capacity and beyond his knowledge; otherwise, acting on error is obligatory and acting on truth forbidden. And it was answered by choosing the second alternative and denying the invalidity of the consequent — i.e., that acting on error is obligatory, as in the case where a definitive proof is hidden from him and he makes ijtihad contrary to it, he must act on it until it becomes apparent, even though it is an error — by consensus. The secret is that the command, by His eternal mercy, is easy, and it consists of restricting action to opinion, whether it corresponds or not; and the aim of His regard is sincerity and obedience in heart. Understand.
Source pages p. 421

Commentary
This passage presents the first proof of the musawwibun (those who hold every mujtahid is correct) against the mukhatti'un (those who hold only one is correct). They argue: if only one mujtahid is correct, then the mistaken one faces a dilemma: either he is obligated to follow the truth (impossible because he does not know it) or he is obligated to follow his error (leading to contradiction — error is wrong, yet he must act on it). The Hanafi response chooses the second horn, arguing that it is indeed obligatory for him to act on his ijtihad even if it turns out to be in error. This is not contradictory because the Shari'ah sometimes orders action on opinion, as in the case of a hidden definitive text: the mujtahid must act on his ijtihad until the truth becomes apparent, even though he is mistaken. The rationale is that Allah's command is based on mercy, limiting obligation to what one can reach through opinion, and the focus is on sincerity and obedience of the heart, not infallible correctness.

Key Terms
تكليف بما لا وسع له (taklīf bi-mā lā wusʿa lahu): A burden beyond one's capacity; here, burdening the mistaken mujtahid with the truth he does not know.
المصوّبون (al-muṣawwibūn): Those who hold that every mujtahid is correct (i.e., every ijtihad arrives at the truth for that mujtahid).
المخطّئون (al-mukhaṭṭiʾūn): Those who hold that only one mujtahid's opinion is correct in reality, but the mistaken is excused.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi response to the Musawwibun's first proof, what is the ruling for a mujtahid who makes ijtihad contrary to a hidden definitive text?
A) He is sinful and must stop acting on his ijtihad
B) He must act on his ijtihad until the text becomes apparent
C) He is obligated to discover the truth even by supernatural means
D) He must follow the opinion of the majority

Key Takeaway
The mistaken mujtahid is obligated to act on his ijtihad; the Shari'ah commands action based on accessible opinion, not hidden truth, out of mercy.

Tarjih between Conflicting Ijtihads: The Hanafi Position
Concluding section on ijtihad and taqlid: tarjih

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule of tarjih when two ijtihads conflict and distinguish the Hanafi position from the musawwibun view.

Core Idea
When ijtihads conflict, tarjih is necessary because only one ijtihad corresponds to reality.

This concluding passage from a Hanafi usul text clarifies a critical rule: when mujtahids disagree, a judge must determine which ijtihad is preponderant (tarjih) and implement that. This flows from the Hanafi conviction that only one ijtihad correctly matches divine reality. The passage contrasts this with the musawwibun view, where every ijtihad is considered correct, leading to logical contradictions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two schools of ijtihad epistemology
The Hanafi (mukhatti'a) holds that only one ijtihad is correct in reality; the musawwiba holds that all ijtihads are correct for their practitioners.
2
State the Hanafi rule when ijtihads conflict
When two ijtihads conflict in a single case (e.g., in court), a judge must perform tarjih to choose the preponderant ruling and act on it.
3
Explain the practical consequence of each position
Under musawwiba, the same action could be both halal and haram simultaneously, which is impossible. Under Hanafi, conflict is resolved by tarjih.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
خاتمة في الاجتهاد والتقليد يترجح أحد الاجتهادين فيعمل. وأما عند هؤلاء فاجتهاد كل مطابق للواقع فيجتمع الحل والحرمة، أو الحل لاثنين في زمان واحد قطعاً، بخلاف ما نحن فيه. فإنّ أحد الاجتهادين خطأ في الواقع، وإنما كان لنا العمل بكل انفراداً، وإذا اجتمعا فبترجيح آخر فافهم.
Conclusion on ijtihad and taqlid: One of the two ijtihads becomes preponderant and is acted upon. As for according to these (the musawwibun), each ijtihad corresponds to reality, so halal and haram, or permissibility to two men, would coincide in one time definitely, unlike our position. For one of the two ijtihads is erroneous in reality, but we act on each individually; when they conflict, we use another preponderance. Understand.
Source pages p. 421

Commentary
This is the concluding section (khatima) on ijtihad and taqlid from the Hanafi perspective. It restates the core difference between the Hanafi (mukhatti'un) and the musawwibun: for the Hanafis, only one mujtahid's ruling is correct in reality; the other is erroneous. However, each mujtahid must act on his own ijtihad individually. When they conflict in a single case, a judge must determine preponderance (tarjih) and act on that. In contrast, for the musawwibun, each ijtihad matches reality, leading to impossible consequences like the same woman being halal and haram simultaneously. The conclusion emphasizes that for us (Hanafis), one ijtihad is objectively wrong, but it is still binding on the one whose ijtihad it is. When two ijtihads meet (e.g., in court), a further process of tarjih selects one to implement.

Key Terms
الترجيح (al-tarjīḥ): The process of determining which of two conflicting ijtihads is preponderant and should be followed in practice.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the conclusion, what happens when two mujtahids' rulings conflict in a practical case according to the Hanafis?
A) Both rulings are implemented separately
B) A judge determines which is preponderant and acts on it
C) The two rulings are considered equally valid
D) The case is referred to a higher mujtahid

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, when ijtihads conflict, tarjih is mandatory to resolve the dispute because only one ijtihad is true in reality.

Rulings on Taqlid for a Mujtahid
Presenting the debate on whether a mujtahid may follow another mujtahid

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Ḥanafī majority position on a mujtahid following another mujtahid before ijtihād, and evaluate the key arguments (dalīl al-ibāḥa al-aṣliyya, qiyās with wuḍūʾ/tayammum, the hadith of the stars) used in the dispute.

Core Idea
A mujtahid must rely on his own ijtihād; taqlīd is a substitute permitted only when ijtihād is impossible.

This lesson explores a foundational uṣūlī debate: can one who is capable of ijtihād (independent reasoning) simply follow the opinion of another mujtahid before exerting his own effort? The Ḥanafī majority, as presented in this passage, firmly prohibits it, drawing a powerful analogy between ijtihād/wuḍūʾ and taqlīd/tayammum. We will walk through the three main arguments of the majority, examine the counterarguments, and understand why the Ḥanafī position has prevailed.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal issue and the spectrum of opinions
The passage first addresses an uncontroversial point: after a mujtahid has performed ijtihād, he cannot abandon his own conclusion. The core dispute is about the situation before ijtihād—can he still follow another? List the opinions: absolute permission, absolute prohibition (majority), prohibition except when fearing loss of the act, and permission only for fatwā not personal practice.
Note the two narrations from Abū Ḥanīfa and the view of Muḥammad al-Shaybānī that one should follow the most knowledgeable (a kind of ijtihād).
2
Analyze the majority's first argument: permissibility needs a proof
The majority argue that the permissibility of taqlīd for a mujtahid is a legal ruling and therefore requires a specific proof (dalīl). Since none exists, the ruling must be prohibition. The opposing side invokes the principle of 'original permissibility' (ibāḥa aṣliyya)—that in the absence of a proof, things are permitted.
This step highlights a key uṣūlī clash: the role of the 'original state' before revelation. The majority counters that ibāḥa aṣliyya applies to worldly matters, not to acts of worship or legal rulings where a duty exists.
3
Examine the central analogy: ijtihād as aṣl, taqlīd as badal
The second argument is a qiyās: ijtihād is like wuḍūʾ (the original method of purification) and taqlīd is like tayammum (a substitute resorted to only when the original is impossible). Therefore, a mujtahid must perform ijtihād and cannot choose taqlīd. Understand the illa: both are the obligatory primary means to an end, and the substitute is only allowed when the original is unattainable.
The response from the opposing side denies that taqlīd is a substitute; they claim both are 'originals'. The author dismisses this as dialectical and reinforces the analogy by noting that the conjecture (ẓann) from ijtihād is stronger than any from taqlīd.
4
Analyze the third argument: symmetry between before and after ijtihād
If taqlīd were allowed before ijtihād, it would also be allowed after—because the faculty of ijtihād exists in both states. But after ijtihād it is prohibited by consensus, so it must also be prohibited before. The response shifts the obstacle from the faculty to the actual attainment of the stronger conjecture.
This step clarifies that the prohibition is not about capability but about the duty to act on the strongest available evidence. Once ijtihād produces a ruling, it must be followed.
5
Evaluate the hadith of the stars and conclude
The passage finally addresses the famous hadith 'My Companions are like stars' used to justify taqlīd of Companions. The author dismisses it as weak and even if authentic, it addresses laypersons, not mujtahids, and only allows following a Companion, not later scholars. The takeaway: the Ḥanafī majority position is prohibition, and its arguments are methodologically robust.
This step integrates the 'expansion probe' nature of the passage: the author expands the dispute by showing various opinions and then critiques them, leaving the strong majority view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(وما صح من مذهب أبي حنيفة أن القاضي المجتهد لو قضى بغير رأيه ذكراً له نفذ هذه رواية أخرى لا ينفذ وفي صورة النسيان ينفذ رواية واحدة خلافاً لصاحبيه) في الوجهين فلا ينافيه لأن النفاذ على تقدير الفعل لا ولم يوجد فلا يوجد الجواز، لأن ما لا دليل عليه شرعاً يجب نفيه شرعاً وأجيب بأنه أي الدليل الإباحة (الأصلية فإنه قد علم من الشريعة أن ما لم يقم عليه دليل فهو مباح بخلاف تحريمكم) فإنه لا بد له من دليل بخصوصه (و) للأكثر (ثانياً : الاجتهاد أصل كالوضوء، والتقليد بدل كالتيمم ولا يرتكب البدل، إلا عند تعذر الأصل، فلا يختار التقليد إلا عند تعذر الاجتهاد (قيل) لا نسلم أن التقليد بدل بل كل منهما (أصل) للعمل كذا في شرح المختصر . أقول : لا يخفى أنه جدل لا (فإن القادر على اليقين يسمع ممنوع من الظن كذلك القادر على الظن الأقوى ممنوع من الظن الأضعف والفرق تحكم والظن الحاصل بالاجتهاد أقوى من الظن بقول غيره، بل للتخفيف (و) للأكثر (ثالثاً : لو جاز التقليد (قبله لجاز ،بعده إذ لا مانع منه إلا ملكة الاجتهاد) وهي متحققة في الصورتين والجواز بعده باطل إجماعاً، فكذا قبله (وأجيب) كون المانع الاجتهاد ممنوع (بل) المانع حصول أقوى الظنين بالفعل بل فيه ترك لما يظنه حكماً إلهياً، يقول رجل : أتباع الشافعي (قالوا) قال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم: أصحابي كالنجوم فبأيهم اقتديتم اهتديتم» (قلنا) هذا الحديث مضعف ولو ثبت فخطاب للمقلد) كما مر على أنه مستلزم لجزء المدعي) وهو جواز تقليد الصحابي لا للجزء الآخر، كما أنه
Issue: The mujtahid after his ijtihad and knowledge of the ruling is prohibited from taqlid in it by consensus, because what he knows to be Allah's ruling he does not abandon for the saying of anyone. If you say: Did not Imam Abu Hanifa rule that a judge's decision contrary to his own ijtihad is enforceable? So where is the consensus? He said: What is established from the madhhab of Abu Hanifa is that if a mujtahid judge passes judgment contrary to his own opinion deliberately, it is not enforceable in one narration; in the case of forgetfulness, it is enforceable in one narration, contrary to his two companions in both aspects. So it does not contradict because enforceability on the assumption of the act does not entail its permissibility. So the prohibition is agreed upon. And know that what is mentioned in al-Hidayah and others is that the fatwa is according to them in both cases. As for before ijtihad, some said it is absolutely permissible, and the majority said it is absolutely prohibited. And it is said it is prohibited unless one fears missing the opportunity, and upon this is Ibn Surayj, and my opinion is that it is an interpretation of the previous saying. And it is said it is prohibited in what one gives fatwa on, not for his own practice. And from Imam Abu Hanifa there are two narrations: in one it is permissible, in another not. And from Imam Muhammad: he follows one who is more knowledgeable than him, which is a kind of ijtihad because it requires reflection on men to know the most knowledgeable. And Imam al-Shafi'i (may Allah have mercy on him) and al-Jubba'i (the Mu'tazili) said it is permissible if the one being followed is a Companion. And it is said it is permissible if he is a Companion or a Tabi'i. And it is said: one follows only the two shaykhs, the best of the Siddiqin after the prophets (peace be upon them), Abu Bakr al-Siddiq and Amir al-Mu'minin 'Umar (may Allah be pleased with them both), to the exclusion of others. The majority hold prohibition. First (argument of the majority): permissibility is a legal ruling, so it requires a proof, and no proof exists, so permissibility does not exist, because what has no legal proof must be negated legally. It was answered that the proof is original permissibility; it is known from the Shari'a that what is not established by a proof is permissible, contrary to your prohibition. Second (argument of the majority): ijtihad is an original like wudu', and taqlid is a substitute like tayammum; a substitute is not resorted to except when the original is impossible, so taqlid is not chosen except when ijtihad is impossible. It was said: we do not accept that taqlid is a substitute; rather each is an original for action, as in Sharh al-Mukhtasar. I say: it is clear that this is dialectical, for one capable of certainty is prohibited from conjecture; similarly, one capable of stronger conjecture is prohibited from weaker conjecture, and the distinction is arbitrary. The conjecture obtained through ijtihad is stronger than the conjecture from another's statement; indeed, often no conjecture is found at all in taqlid. And substitution is established by the generality of His statement: 'So take warning, O people of insight' (59:2); for 'taking warning' is obligatory by this text on everyone, so permitting taqlid is a substitute for it and a concession for ease. Third (argument of the majority): if taqlid were permissible before ijtihad, it would be permissible after it, because there is no obstacle except the faculty of ijtihad, and that exists in both cases; and permissibility after ijtihad is invalid by consensus, so likewise before. It was answered that the obstacle is not ijtihad, but rather the actual attainment of the stronger of the two conjectures; indeed, he is abandoning what he deems to be the divine ruling. A man said: The followers of al-Shafi'i said: The Messenger of Allah said: 'My Companions are like stars; by whichever of them you follow, you will be guided.' We replied: This hadith is weak, and even if authentic, it is addressed to the layperson, as previously mentioned; moreover, it entails only part of the claim — namely the permissibility of following a Companion, not the other part.
Source pages p. 426

Commentary
This passage from the khātima (conclusion) of the usul work discusses the ruling on a mujtahid engaging in taqlid (following another mujtahid). The author states that after a mujtahid has performed ijtihad and reached a ruling, he is prohibited by consensus from abandoning his own ruling to follow someone else. This is because he knows the ruling of Allah through his ijtihad, and it is not permissible to leave it for another's opinion. An objection is raised: Did not Imam Abu Hanifa hold that a judge's decision contrary to his own ijtihad is enforceable? The author explains that this is not a permission but merely a ruling on enforceability; the judge is still sinful for doing so. The consensus is on the prohibition. Then the passage addresses the situation before ijtihad: can a person who is capable of ijtihad follow another? Several opinions are recorded: some say it is absolutely permissible; the majority say it is absolutely prohibited; a third opinion says it is prohibited unless one fears missing the time of the act; a fourth says it is prohibited in giving fatwas but not for personal practice. From Imam Abu Hanifa there are two narrations. Imam Muhammad says one may follow a more knowledgeable person, which itself requires a form of ijtihad to identify who is more knowledgeable. Imam al-Shafi'i and al-Jubba'i permit following a Companion only; others extend to Tabi'un; some restrict to Abu Bakr and 'Umar alone. The majority (the 'akthar') give three arguments: (1) Permissibility of taqlid is a legal ruling that requires a proof; there is no proof, so it must be prohibited. The rebuttal from the opposite side is that the original principle is permissibility (ibaha asliyya). (2) Ijtihad is the original method, taqlid is a substitute like tayammum; one cannot resort to a substitute when the original is possible. The response is that some deny taqlid being a substitute, holding both as originals. The author then clarifies that this is mere debate; the stronger conjecture from ijtihad is better than any conjecture from taqlid, and often taqlid yields no conjecture at all. He argues that substitution is proven by the Quranic verse 59:2, implying that ijtihad (i'tibar) is obligatory, and taqlid is a concession. (3) If taqlid were allowed before ijtihad, it would be allowed after, but after is prohibited by consensus, so before also. The response: the obstacle is not the faculty but the actual attainment of stronger conjecture. The final part deals with the hadith of the stars: the author says it is weak, and even if accepted, it only permits following a Companion, not other scholars, and it is meant for laypersons, not mujtahids.

Key Terms
تقليد (taqlid): Following the opinion of a mujtahid without knowing his evidence.
اجتهاد (ijtihad): The utmost effort in deriving legal rulings from the primary sources.
إباحة أصلية (ibāḥa aṣliyya): The original legal presumption that in the absence of a specific ruling, acts are permissible.
الظن الأقوى (al-ẓann al-aqwā): The stronger conjecture (preponderant probability) that results from ijtihad, as opposed to the weaker conjecture from taqlid.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the majority of Hanafis as presented in this passage, why is a mujtahid prohibited from taqlid even before he has performed ijtihad?
A) Because taqlid is always prohibited regardless of the person.
B) Because ijtihad is like wudu' (original) and taqlid is like tayammum (substitute), and one cannot use a substitute when the original is possible.
C) Because the hadith 'My Companions are like stars' is weak.
D) Because Abu Hanifa explicitly prohibited it in all cases.

Key Takeaway
A mujtahid must use his own ijtihad; taqlid is only for those incapable of ijtihad, as the substitute cannot replace the original duty.

Renewal of Ijtihad for Recurring Incidents
Dispute on renewal of ijtihad for recurring incidents

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the dispute on whether renewal of ijtihad is required for a recurring incident and apply the Hanafi principle of istishab to determine the preferred view.

Core Idea
The occurrence of the incident, not the mere possibility of change, triggers ijtihad; istishab preserves the previous ruling.

One of the key questions in usul al-fiqh is whether a mujtahid must re-examine a ruling when the same legal incident occurs again. This passage presents a debate between scholars who require renewal and those who deem the previous ijtihad sufficient. The Hanafi preference for istishab (presumption of continuity) offers a principled resolution grounded in legal cause (sabab).

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue and the two main positions
State the question: if an incident recurs after ijtihad, must the mujtahid renew his consideration? Note the view of Ibn al-Hajib (no renewal) and al-Qadi Abu Bakr (yes renewal).
2
Analyze the cause (sabab) for renewal
Determine what triggers the obligation to renew: is it the mere possibility of change (al-ihtimal) or the actual occurrence of the incident? The text argues the cause is the occurrence, not possibility.
This step is critical for the Hanafi argument against perpetual renewal.
3
Apply the principle of istishab
Understand that istishab presumes continuity of the previous ruling unless strong evidence of change appears. The mere possibility does not rebut istishab.
Istishab is a fundamental Hanafi principle used to avoid unnecessary repetition.
4
Evaluate the analogy from the Prophet's time
Consider the example of travelers returning to Medina not being asked about abrogation. This illustrates that mere possibility of change does not require action.
The analogy supports the view that renewal is not obligatory.
5
Note the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf)
The dispute has real consequences: if renewal is required, a mujtahid must constantly re-derive rulings for recurrent cases; if not, he relies on his previous work unless evidence emerges.
The preferred Hanafi position reduces burden and maintains consistency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة : إذا تكررت الواقعة وقد اجتهد فيها قبل وعرف حكمها فهل يجب تجديد النظر فيها قيل : لا يجب، بل يكفي النظر السابق واختاره ابن الحاجب لأنه يجاب بلا موجب شرعي وقيل : نعم يجب وعليه القاضي أبو بكر لأن الاجتهاد كثيراً ما يتغير فلاحتمال التغير يجب التجديد لتظهر حقيقة الحال قيل إذا كان التجديد لهذا فيجب تكريره أبداً لدوام الاحتمال احتمال التغير ولا يخفى ضعفه، لأن السبب لتجديد النظر وقوع الواقعة لا احتمال التغير وهو أي وقوع الواقعة لا يدوم فلا يدوم التكرار بل الجواب الحق أن الظاهر الاستصحاب وبقاء الاجتهاد وبالاحتمال لا يجب شيء كما كان في الزمان الشريف لا يجب استفسار من كان يجيء من السفر وفي المدينة أن هذا الحكم هل انتسخ أم لا وقيل: إن كان ذاكراً للدليل الأول عند تكرر الحادثة فلا يجب التجديد وإلا فنعم يجب وعليه الآمدي والنووي ولا يظهر للتذكر دخل، فإن النظر من المعدات التي لا يجب وجودها مع المطلوب فتذكر المطلوب كاف واحتمال التغير باق في الحالين فتأمل وفي العامي إذا استفتى مرة ثم تكرر الواقعة فهل يلزمه السؤال ثانياً فيه قولان فعند قائل يجب، وعند آخر لا .
Issue: If the incident recurs and he had performed ijtihad before and knew its ruling, is it obligatory to renew the consideration? It is said: Not obligatory, rather the previous consideration suffices, and Ibn al-Hajib chose this because answering without a legal cause is required. And it is said: Yes, it is obligatory, and al-Qadi Abu Bakr held this, because ijtihad often changes, so due to the possibility of change, renewal is obligatory to reveal the true state. It is said: If renewal is for this, then it must be repeated perpetually because of the continuation of the possibility of change. Its weakness is clear, because the cause for renewal of consideration is the occurrence of the incident, not the possibility of change. And the occurrence of the incident does not continue, so repetition does not continue. Rather, the correct answer is that the apparent is istishab (continuity) and the continuation of the ijtihad, and by mere possibility nothing is obligatory, just as in the noble era it was not obligatory to question someone coming from travel in Medina whether this ruling had been abrogated or not. And it is said: If he remembers the first evidence when the incident recurs, then renewal is not obligatory; otherwise, it is obligatory. Al-Amidi and al-Nawawi held this. The relevance of memory is not apparent, because consideration is among the preparatory means which are not required to be present when the objective is achieved; thus remembering the objective is sufficient, and the possibility of change remains in both cases, so reflect. And regarding the layman, if he asks for a fatwa once and then the incident recurs, is he obligated to ask again? There are two opinions: one says yes, another says no.
Source pages p. 427

Commentary
This passage addresses the question of whether a mujtahid must renew his ijtihad (personal reasoning) when a legal incident repeats itself after he has already derived a ruling for it. Two main opinions are presented. The first holds that no renewal is required; the previous ijtihad is sufficient, as chosen by Ibn al-Hajib, because requiring renewal without a specific legal cause would be arbitrary. The second opinion, held by al-Qadi Abu Bakr al-Baqillani, argues that renewal is obligatory because ijtihad is subject to change over time; the ruling derived earlier may not be the same now, so to ensure accuracy, the mujtahid must reconsider. An objection is raised: if the reason for renewal is the mere possibility of change, then renewal would be required perpetually, which is impractical. The response clarifies that the cause for renewal is the occurrence of the incident itself, not the possibility of change. Since the incident does not occur continuously, renewal is not perpetual. The preferred view is that the principle of istishab (presumption of continuity) applies; the previous ijtihad remains valid until there is strong evidence of a change. The mere possibility does not obligate action. An analogy is given: in the time of the Prophet, a traveler returning to Medina was not asked whether the ruling had been abrogated. A third opinion ties renewal to memory: if the mujtahid remembers the first evidence, no renewal; if he forgets, he must renew. But this is criticized because memory does not affect the need for renewal since the real objective is the ruling, and remembering it is sufficient. The probability of change exists whether or not he remembers. Finally, the same question is asked for a layperson: if he receives a fatwa and then the same situation recurs, must he ask again? There are two opinions. This shows the complexity of the issue in Hanafi usul.

Key Terms
تجديد النظر (tajdid al-nazar): Renewal of ijtihad or re-examination of the evidence for a recurring legal incident.
الاستصحاب (al-istishab): Presumption of continuity of a previous state or ruling until evidence of change arises.
الاحتمال (al-ihtimal): Possibility, here referring to the possibility that the ruling may have changed due to new evidence or circumstances.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the preferred view in this passage, what is the basis for not requiring renewal of ijtihad for a recurring incident?
A) The principle of istishab (continuity) and that the cause is the occurrence, not possibility.
B) The mujtahid's memory of the evidence.
C) The layperson's need for a new fatwa.
D) The consensus of the early scholars.

Key Takeaway
A mujtahid may rely on his previous ijtihad for a recurring case based on istishab, as mere possibility of change does not necessitate renewal.

Renewal of Ijtihad for Recurring Incidents
Usul rule on renewal of ijtihad with multiple views

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi usul principle of istishab to evaluate whether a mujtahid must renew ijtihad for a recurring question.

Core Idea
A previous ijtihad persists by istishab; mere possibility of change does not necessitate renewal.

When a legal question you have already resolved arises again, must you re-examine it? This passage from 'Fawatih al-Rahamut' presents three scholarly positions and defends the Hanafi view that the previous ijtihad suffices, grounded in the principle of istishab. Understanding this debate sharpens your grasp of how usul al-fiqh balances certainty and probability in legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core question
The issue is whether a mujtahid must renew his ijtihad when the same incident recurs after he has already derived a ruling.
2
List the three positions
Extract the three views: (1) No renewal needed (Ibn al-Hajib, author's preference), (2) Renewal obligatory (Qadi Abu Bakr), (3) Renewal only if evidence forgotten (al-Amidi, al-Nawawi).
Each view is based on a different evaluation of the possibility of change.
3
Analyze the proof for the preferred view
The author argues using istishab: the previous ijtihad is presumed to remain valid. The cause for renewal is the recurrence of the incident, not the mere possibility of change. Since recurrence does not persist, endless repetition is avoided.
The analogy from the Prophet's time (not re-questioning travelers) supports this.
4
Evaluate the memory-based position
The view linking renewal to memory of the evidence is criticized: remembering the ruling is sufficient, and the possibility of change exists regardless of memory.
This shows that usul analysis prioritizes the underlying cause over subjective factors.
5
Apply the principle of istishab
Conclude that the Hanafi position relies on istishab of the previous ijtihad. A mujtahid may act on his earlier ruling without renewing ijtihad unless a definite change occurs.
This principle prevents unnecessary repeated exertion and maintains stability in law.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة: إذا تكررت الواقعة وقد اجتهد فيها قبل وعرف حكمها فهل يجب تجديد النظر فيها وقيل: لا يجب، بل يكفي النظر السابق واختاره ابن الحاجب لأنه يجاب بلا موجب شرعي وقيل: نعم يجب وعليه القاضي أبو بكر لأن الاجتهاد كثيراً ما يتغير فلاحتمال التغير يجب التجديد لتظهر حقيقة الحال قيل: إذا كان التجديد لهذا فيجب تكريره أبداً لدوام احتمال التغير ولا يخفى ضعفه، لأن السبب لتجديد النظر وقوع الواقعة لا احتمال التغير وهو أي وقوع الواقعة لا يدوم فلا يدوم التكرار بل الجواب الحق أن الظاهر الاستصحاب وبقاء الاجتهاد وبالاحتمال لا يجب شيء كما كان في الزمان الشريف لا يجب استفسار من كان يجيء من السفر وفي المدينة أن هذا الحكم هل انتسخ أم لا وقيل: إن كان ذاكراً للدليل الأول عند تكرر الحادثة فلا يجب التجديد وإلا فنعم يجب وعليه الآمدي والنووي ولا يظهر للتذكر دخل، فإن النظر من المعدات التي لا يجب وجودها مع المطلوب فتذكر المطلوب كاف واحتمال التغير باق في الحالين فتأمل
Issue: If an incident recurs and he has previously exercised ijtihad and known its ruling, must he renew consideration? Some said: No, the previous consideration suffices, chosen by Ibn al-Hajib because it would be answered without a legal cause. Some said: Yes, it is obligatory, held by Qadi Abu Bakr because ijtihad often changes, so due to possibility of change renewal is obligatory to reveal the true situation. It was said: If renewal is for this reason, it must be repeated forever due to the continuity of the possibility of change, but its weakness is obvious because the cause for renewing consideration is the occurrence of the incident, not the possibility of change, and the occurrence does not persist, so repetition does not persist. Rather the correct answer is that the apparent is istishab (presumption of continuity) and the persistence of ijtihad, and with mere possibility nothing becomes obligatory, just as in the noble time it was not obligatory to ask again of one who came from travel. And in the city whether this ruling was abrogated or not. And it was said: If he remembers the first evidence when the incident recurs, renewal is not obligatory; otherwise it is, held by al-Amidi and al-Nawawi. But the role of memory is not apparent, for consideration is among the means that need not exist with the desired result; remembering the desired result is sufficient, and the possibility of change remains in both cases, so ponder.
Source pages p. 427

Commentary
This passage discusses whether a mujtahid must renew his ijtihad when the same legal question arises again. There are three views: (1) No renewal needed, based on the principle of istishab (presumption of continuity) and that the previous ijtihad remains valid. (2) Yes, renewal is obligatory, due to the possibility that the mujtahid's opinion may have changed. (3) Renewal is only necessary if he does not remember the evidence from the previous ijtihad. The author prefers the first view, arguing that the mere possibility of change does not necessitate renewal, and that the cause for renewal is the recurrence of the incident, not the possibility of change, and recurrence does not persist. He supports this with the example of the Prophet's time where those coming from travel were not re-questioned. The view dependent on memory is criticized because the purpose is the ruling, not the evidence, and possibility of change exists whether he remembers or not. The Hanafi principle here is that ijtihad, once established, is presumed to persist until a definite change occurs.

Key Terms
استصحاب (istishab): Presumption of continuity of a previously established state until a change is certain.
اجتهاد (ijtihad): Exertion to derive a ruling; here it refers to the outcome of such effort.
تجديد النظر (tajdid al-nazar): Renewal of consideration or ijtihad for a ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The mujtahid's memory of the evidence
B) The principle of istishab
C) The absence of a qualified mujtahid
D) The consensus of the Companions

Key Takeaway
A previous ijtihad persists by istishab; mere possibility of change does not obligate renewal.
